--- LOOP 1 REVISED TEXT --- 


    #### 1.1 Evolution of Projection Architectures

The architectural design of the modality interface—the bridge that connects visual encoders with Large Language Models (LLMs)—has evolved into a central battleground for defining the capabilities of Multimodal Large Language Models (MLLMs) <30613394>. While early efforts, such as <Visual Instruction Tuning>, demonstrated the viability of connecting a visual encoder to an LLM using a simple linear projection layer, the field has rapidly shifted toward more sophisticated architectures. These newer designs aim to handle the "modality gap" more effectively, resulting in a spectrum of interfaces ranging from specialized compression modules to deep, interleaved fusion strategies.

A prominent approach involves the use of specialized modules to map visual tokens into the semantic space of the LLM. <MiniGPT-4> utilizes Q-Former (inspired by BLIP-2) to learn a set of learnable queries (typically 32) that aggregate visual features from a frozen ViT before they are fed into the LLM. This method proved that treating visual input as a sequence of compressed, highly relevant features allows an otherwise frozen LLM to understand complex modalities, unlocking emergent capabilities like website generation from sketches. Similarly, <KOSMOS-1> adopts a Perceiver-like architecture to handle interleaved multimodal inputs within a single Transformer. By employing a mapping network and special boundary tokens, it effectively unifies modalities. However, models relying on complex, pre-trained feature compressors like Q-Former (as seen in <BLIVA>, which integrates Q-Former-style embeddings with patch embeddings to better handle text-rich images) often introduce significant parameter overhead and latency. This trade-off between compression efficiency and computational cost defines a key tension in early connector designs.

In reaction to this complexity, recent research has sought to simplify the interface without sacrificing performance. <mPLUG-Owl2> introduces a "modality-adaptive module" that preserves modality-specific features, arguing that strict fusion can degrade text-only capabilities. It decouples the visual pathway while still sharing a universal language decoder. Conversely, <FIBER> argues for "Fusion in the Backbone." Instead of a separate connector, it interleaves cross-attention layers directly into the LLM’s encoder and decoder context. This deep fusion allows the model to reason about visual dependencies dynamically throughout the generation process, rather than relying on a static pre-fusion step. This trade-off—between specialized, compressed encoders (<MiniGPT-4>) and deep, integrated fusion (<FIBER>)—defines the current tension in interface design.

Simultaneously, a radical shift in philosophy has emerged, epitomized by <VisionLLM>. This work proposes treating images as a "foreign language." Rather than designing complex connectors, it aligns vision-centric tasks directly with the LLM’s language processing capabilities. It uses a language-guided image tokenizer and treats outputs (like bounding boxes) as text tokens, effectively unifying the architecture into a pure generative text decoder. This approach suggests that the modality interface might eventually disappear entirely, with the LLM handling all modalities natively. <GIT> further supports this generative unification, achieving state-of-the-art performance in image-to-text generation with a simple encoder-decoder architecture that requires no complex modality-specific modules. This evolution from simple linear projections (<Visual Instruction Tuning>) to specialized compressors (<MiniGPT-4>, <KOSMOS-1>), deep fusion (<FIBER>, <mPLUG-Owl2>), and finally generative unification (<VisionLLM>, <GIT>) represents the field’s search for the most efficient way to bridge the visual and linguistic worlds.

However, architectural innovation cannot compensate for insufficient training data, a point emphasized by <Static Augmentation>. This paper argues that standard supervised fine-tuning (SFT) data is often too narrow, limiting the model's ability to generalize across different instruction styles. This highlights that the "interface" is not just architecture; it is also the instructional data that teaches the model how to utilize that architecture. Even the most elegant connector, such as the "Spatial Vision Aggregator (SVA)" proposed in <Cambrian-1>, relies on a massive, high-quality dataset to fine-tune the interaction between visual backbones and the LLM.

Beyond the internal fusion strategies discussed above, the industry is also trending toward architectures that prioritize "visual grounding"—explicitly linking text to visual regions. <KOSMOS-2> introduced grounding capabilities by training on grounded image-text pairs, allowing the model to output <region> tags corresponding to bounding boxes. While not strictly an architectural connector change, it alters the output interface to enforce alignment between the visual and textual streams. Similarly, <UniTAB> Unifies Text and Box outputs, proposing a shared token sequence that allows for grounded captioning. The inclusion of an <obj> token represents a structural change to the interface, enabling the model to explicitly align generated words with visual locations.

The trajectory of research suggests that while early interfaces focused on "how to connect" (via linear layers or projectors), the current focus is on "how to integrate." <mPLUG-Owl2> demonstrates that preserving modality-specific features is crucial for general intelligence, while <FIBER> suggests that dynamic fusion is key for fine-grained tasks. As we look at the evolution from <Visual Instruction Tuning>'s linear layer to <VisionLLM>'s generative decoder, the interface is transforming from a static bridge into a dynamic, intelligent router that determines which modality to prioritize and how to fuse context effectively.

The limitations of current interfaces are also becoming apparent in specific domains. For example, <BLIVA> addresses the failure of generalist connectors in text-rich images by explicitly injecting patch embeddings alongside learned query embeddings. This suggests that "one size fits all" interfaces may not be optimal. As the line between the visual encoder and the language model blurs, exemplified by <Downscaling Intelligence>'s proposal for visual extraction tuning, the modality interface is effectively becoming part of the reasoning engine itself, rather than a mere data conduit.

In conclusion, the modality interface has moved from a trivial linear layer to a core architectural component defining the model's reasoning capabilities. The choice between heavy, pre-trained connectors and deep, integrated fusion reflects a fundamental debate on whether visual processing should be heuristically compressed before reaching the LLM or treated as an integral part of the reasoning process. However, as highlighted by the 'one size fits all' limitations discussed in <BLIVA>, the field is increasingly recognizing that a single architectural approach may be insufficient. Works like <VisionLLM> and <GIT> suggest the ultimate interface may be the absence of one—a unified generative space—while current practical architectures like <Cambrian-1> and <FIBER> are pushing the boundaries of sophisticated, multi-stage fusion to maximize performance on complex reasoning tasks.

    #### 1.1 Evolution of Projection Architectures

The architectural design of the modality interface—the bridge that connects visual encoders with Large Language Models (LLMs)—has evolved into a central battleground for defining the capabilities of Multimodal Large Language Models (MLLMs) <MME: A Comprehensive Evaluation Benchmark for Multimodal Large Language Models>. While early efforts, such as <Visual Instruction Tuning>, demonstrated the viability of connecting a visual encoder to an LLM using a simple linear projection layer, the field has rapidly shifted toward more sophisticated architectures. These newer designs aim to handle the "modality gap"—the significant discrepancy in feature distributions and semantic granularity between visual and linguistic modalities <2306.13394>—more effectively, resulting in a spectrum of interfaces ranging from specialized compression modules to deep, interleaved fusion strategies. This evolution reflects a broader taxonomy of projection architectures, generally categorized into: (1) Linear/Projector-based approaches, (2) Q-Former based learnable query architectures, (3) Cross-Attention based fusion mechanisms, and (4) Token-based or direct injection methods. This section first examines connector-based approaches, then discusses deep fusion strategies, and finally analyzes the trend toward architectural unification.

#### 1.1.1 Connector-Based Architectures

A prominent approach involves the use of specialized modules to map visual tokens into the semantic space of the LLM. <MiniGPT-4> utilizes Q-Former (inspired by BLIP-2) to learn a set of learnable queries (typically 32) that aggregate visual features from a frozen ViT before they are fed into the LLM. This method proved that treating visual input as a sequence of compressed, highly relevant features allows an otherwise frozen LLM to understand complex modalities, unlocking emergent capabilities like website generation from sketches. Similarly, <KOSMOS-1> adopts a Perceiver-like architecture to handle interleaved multimodal inputs within a single Transformer. By employing a mapping network and special boundary tokens, it effectively unifies modalities. However, reliance on complex, pre-trained feature compressors introduces significant parameter overhead and latency. For instance, <BLIVA> integrates Q-Former-style embeddings with patch embeddings to better handle text-rich images, but this design adds computational cost. This trade-off between compression efficiency and computational cost defines a key tension in early connector designs.

#### 1.1.2 Deep Fusion and Unification

In reaction to this complexity, recent research has sought to simplify the interface without sacrificing performance. <mPLUG-Owl2> introduces a "modality-adaptive module" that preserves modality-specific features, arguing that strict fusion can degrade text-only capabilities. It decouples the visual pathway while still sharing a universal language decoder. Conversely, <FIBER> argues for "Fusion in the Backbone." Instead of a separate connector, it interleaves cross-attention layers directly into the LLM’s encoder and decoder context. This deep fusion allows the model to reason about visual dependencies dynamically throughout the generation process, rather than relying on a static pre-fusion step. This trade-off—between specialized, compressed encoders (<MiniGPT-4>) and deep, integrated fusion (<FIBER>)—defines the current tension in interface design.

Simultaneously, a radical shift in philosophy has emerged, epitomized by <VisionLLM>. This work proposes treating images as a "foreign language." Rather than designing complex connectors, it aligns vision-centric tasks directly with the LLM’s language processing capabilities. It uses a language-guided image tokenizer and treats outputs (like bounding boxes) as text tokens, effectively unifying the architecture into a pure generative text decoder. This approach suggests that the modality interface might eventually disappear entirely, with the LLM handling all modalities natively. <GIT> further supports this generative unification, achieving state-of-the-art performance in image-to-text generation with a simple encoder-decoder architecture that requires no complex modality-specific modules. This evolution from simple linear projections (<Visual Instruction Tuning>) to specialized compressors (<MiniGPT-4>, <KOSMOS-1>), deep fusion (<FIBER>, <mPLUG-Owl2>), and finally generative unification (<VisionLLM>, <GIT>) represents the field’s search for the most efficient way to bridge the visual and linguistic worlds.

However, the efficacy of these architectural designs is intrinsically linked to the training data they require. For instance, the "Spatial Vision Aggregator (SVA)" proposed in <Cambrian-1> is a sophisticated connector that relies on a massive, high-quality dataset to fine-tune the interaction between visual backbones and the LLM. SVA typically functions by employing spatial pooling or attention mechanisms to aggregate high-resolution visual features into a compact set of tokens suitable for the LLM. This illustrates a broader trend: as architectures evolve from simple linear layers to complex, deep fusion mechanisms, they often demand more specialized and extensive training data to unlock their full potential, a challenge highlighted by studies on data scaling like <Static Augmentation>.

Beyond the internal fusion strategies discussed above, the industry is also trending toward architectures that prioritize "visual grounding"—explicitly linking text to visual regions. <KOSMOS-2> introduced grounding capabilities by training on grounded image-text pairs, allowing the model to output <region> tags corresponding to bounding boxes. While not strictly an architectural connector change, it alters the output interface to enforce alignment between the visual and textual streams. Similarly, <UniTAB> Unifies Text and Box outputs, proposing a shared token sequence that allows for grounded captioning. The inclusion of an <obj> token represents a structural change to the interface, enabling the model to explicitly align generated words with visual locations.

The trajectory of research suggests that while early interfaces focused on "how to connect" (via linear layers or projectors), the current focus is on "how to integrate." <mPLUG-Owl2> demonstrates that preserving modality-specific features is crucial for general intelligence, while <FIBER> suggests that dynamic fusion is key for fine-grained tasks. As we look at the evolution from <Visual Instruction Tuning>'s linear layer to <VisionLLM>'s generative decoder, the interface is transforming from a static bridge into a dynamic, intelligent router that determines which modality to prioritize and how to fuse context effectively.

The limitations of current interfaces are also becoming apparent in specific domains. For example, <BLIVA> addresses the failure of generalist connectors in text-rich images by explicitly injecting patch embeddings alongside learned query embeddings. This suggests that "one size fits all" interfaces may not be optimal. As the line between the visual encoder and the language model blurs, exemplified by <Downscaling Intelligence>'s proposal for visual extraction tuning, the modality interface is effectively becoming part of the reasoning engine itself, rather than a mere data conduit.

In conclusion, the modality interface has moved from a trivial linear layer to a core architectural component defining the model's reasoning capabilities. The choice between heavy, pre-trained connectors and deep, integrated fusion reflects a fundamental debate on whether visual processing should be heuristically compressed before reaching the LLM or treated as an integral part of the reasoning process. However, as highlighted by the 'one size fits all' limitations discussed in <BLIVA>, the field is increasingly recognizing that a single architectural approach may be insufficient. This trade-off is critical: heavy connectors like Q-Former often incur significant latency and parameter overhead, potentially limiting real-time application, whereas deep fusion strategies like those in <FIBER> may offer superior reasoning capabilities but at a higher computational cost per token. Consequently, a central open research question remains: how to design an interface that optimally balances inference efficiency with the rich, dynamic reasoning required for complex tasks, or whether the future lies in the unified generative spaces proposed by <VisionLLM> and <GIT>.

    #### 1.1 Evolution of Modality Interfaces

The architectural design of the modality interface—the bridge that connects visual encoders with Large Language Models (LLMs)—has evolved into a central battleground for defining the capabilities of Multimodal Large Language Models (MLLMs) <MME: A Comprehensive Evaluation Benchmark for Multimodal Large Language Models>. While early efforts, such as <Visual Instruction Tuning>, demonstrated the viability of connecting a visual encoder to an LLM using a simple linear projection layer, the field has rapidly shifted toward more sophisticated architectures. These newer designs aim to handle the "modality gap"—the significant discrepancy in feature distributions and semantic granularity between visual and linguistic modalities <MME: A Comprehensive Evaluation Benchmark for Multimodal Large Language Models>—more effectively, resulting in a spectrum of interfaces ranging from specialized compression modules to deep, interleaved fusion strategies. This evolution reflects a broader taxonomy of projection architectures, generally categorized into: (1) Linear/Projector-based approaches, (2) Q-Former based learnable query architectures, (3) Cross-Attention based fusion mechanisms, and (4) Token-based or direct injection methods. This section first examines connector-based approaches, then discusses deep fusion strategies, and finally analyzes the trend toward architectural unification.

This architectural progression is not merely a design choice but is deeply intertwined with the scaling laws and data efficiency of MLLMs. As highlighted in recent surveys <2306.13549>, the primary goal of these interfaces is to align the representation spaces of vision and language without disrupting the rich semantic knowledge embedded within the frozen LLM. The choice of interface dictates the model's ability to process high-resolution inputs, handle multiple images, and perform complex reasoning. For instance, while linear projectors offer minimal latency, they often struggle with the semantic misalignment, whereas more complex connectors like Q-Former introduce significant parameter overhead but enable better feature compression. The tension between efficiency and expressive power remains a defining characteristic of this field, driving research toward hybrid approaches that seek the best of both worlds.

#### 1.1.1 Linear and Simple Projector Architectures

The foundational approach to bridging the modality gap relies on simple linear projection layers. <Visual Instruction Tuning> first demonstrated the viability of this method by connecting a visual encoder to an LLM using a single linear layer, establishing a baseline for parameter efficiency. This approach aligns visual features into the language embedding space with minimal overhead, though it often struggles with the significant distributional discrepancy between modalities. <LLaVA> further validated this paradigm, showing that such simple projectors, when combined with high-quality instruction tuning data, can yield impressive multimodal capabilities. While highly efficient, these linear methods typically lack the capacity to compress or restructure visual information deeply, often leading to suboptimal performance on complex tasks requiring fine-grained visual understanding.

#### 1.1.2 Connector-Based Architectures

To address the limitations of simple projectors, a prominent approach involves the use of specialized modules to map visual tokens into the semantic space of the LLM. <MiniGPT-4> utilizes Q-Former (inspired by BLIP-2) to learn a set of learnable queries (typically 32) that aggregate visual features from a frozen ViT before they are fed into the LLM. This method proved that treating visual input as a sequence of compressed, highly relevant features allows an otherwise frozen LLM to understand complex modalities, unlocking emergent capabilities like website generation from sketches. Similarly, <KOSMOS-1> adopts a Perceiver-like architecture to handle interleaved multimodal inputs within a single Transformer. This architecture utilizes a cross-attention mechanism to iteratively compress input sequences into a fixed set of latent tokens, allowing it to process variable-length multimodal data efficiently. By employing a mapping network and special boundary tokens, it effectively unifies modalities. However, reliance on complex, pre-trained feature compressors introduces significant parameter overhead and latency. For instance, <BLIVA> integrates Q-Former-style embeddings with patch embeddings to better handle text-rich images, but this design adds computational cost. This trade-off between compression efficiency and computational cost defines a key tension in early connector designs.

#### 1.1.2 Deep Fusion Mechanisms

In reaction to the complexity of specialized connectors, recent research has sought to integrate vision and language more deeply within the model backbone. <mPLUG-Owl2> introduces a "modality-adaptive module" that preserves modality-specific features, arguing that strict fusion can degrade text-only capabilities. It decouples the visual pathway while still sharing a universal language decoder. Conversely, <FIBER> argues for "Fusion in the Backbone." Instead of a separate connector, it interleaves cross-attention layers directly into the LLM’s encoder and decoder context. This deep fusion allows the model to reason about visual dependencies dynamically throughout the generation process, rather than relying on a static pre-fusion step. This trade-off—between specialized, compressed encoders (<MiniGPT-4>) and deep, integrated fusion (<FIBER>)—defines the current tension in interface design.

#### 1.1.3 Unified Generative Architectures

A radical shift in philosophy has emerged, epitomized by <VisionLLM>, which proposes treating images as a "foreign language." Rather than designing complex connectors, it aligns vision-centric tasks directly with the LLM’s language processing capabilities. It uses a language-guided image tokenizer and treats outputs (like bounding boxes) as text tokens, effectively unifying the architecture into a pure generative text decoder. This approach suggests that the modality interface might eventually disappear entirely, with the LLM handling all modalities natively.

<GIT> further supports this generative unification, achieving state-of-the-art performance in image-to-text generation with a simple encoder-decoder architecture that requires no complex modality-specific modules. This evolution from simple linear projections (<Visual Instruction Tuning>) to specialized compressors (<MiniGPT-4>, <KOSMOS-1>), deep fusion (<FIBER>, <mPLUG-Owl2>), and finally generative unification (<VisionLLM>, <GIT>) represents the field’s search for the most efficient way to bridge the visual and linguistic worlds.

#### 1.1.3 Token-based and Direct Injection Methods

Distinct from fusion strategies that mix modalities, token-based or direct injection methods treat visual information as a sequence of tokens directly compatible with the LLM's processing pipeline. This approach often involves mapping visual patches to a discrete codebook or generating visual tokens via a specialized tokenizer, allowing the model to process visual inputs much like text. <EMU> exemplifies this by utilizing a visual tokenizer to convert images into discrete tokens, which are then interleaved with text tokens in a unified sequence for generative modeling. Similarly, early pioneering works like <Flamingo> introduced Perceiver resamplers to aggregate visual features into a fixed number of visual tokens, which are then injected into the language model via cross-attention layers. These methods effectively treat vision as a "foreign language" that the LLM can natively understand, moving beyond simple projection to a unified token space. This strategy simplifies the architecture by removing the need for complex, modality-specific connectors, aligning with the trend toward architectural unification.

However, the efficacy of these architectural designs is intrinsically linked to the training data they require. For instance, the "Spatial Vision Aggregator (SVA)" proposed in <Cambrian-1> is a sophisticated connector that relies on a massive, high-quality dataset to fine-tune the interaction between visual backbones and the LLM. SVA typically functions by employing spatial pooling or attention mechanisms to aggregate high-resolution visual features into a compact set of tokens suitable for the LLM. This illustrates a broader trend: as architectures evolve from simple linear layers to complex, deep fusion mechanisms, they often demand more specialized and extensive training data to unlock their full potential, a challenge highlighted by studies on data scaling like <Static Augmentation>.

Beyond the internal fusion strategies discussed above, the industry is also trending toward architectures that prioritize "visual grounding"—explicitly linking text to visual regions. <KOSMOS-2> introduced grounding capabilities by training on grounded image-text pairs, allowing the model to output <region> tags corresponding to bounding boxes. While not strictly an architectural connector change, it alters the output interface to enforce alignment between the visual and textual streams. Similarly, <UniTAB> Unifies Text and Box outputs, proposing a shared token sequence that allows for grounded captioning. The inclusion of an <obj> token represents a structural change to the interface, enabling the model to explicitly align generated words with visual locations.

The trajectory of research suggests that while early interfaces focused on "how to connect" (via linear layers or projectors), the current focus is on "how to integrate." <mPLUG-Owl2> demonstrates that preserving modality-specific features is crucial for general intelligence, while <FIBER> suggests that dynamic fusion is key for fine-grained tasks. As we look at the evolution from <Visual Instruction Tuning>'s linear layer to <VisionLLM>'s generative decoder, the interface is transforming from a static bridge into a dynamic, intelligent router that determines which modality to prioritize and how to fuse context effectively.

The limitations of current interfaces are also becoming apparent in specific domains. For example, <BLIVA> addresses the failure of generalist connectors in text-rich images by explicitly injecting patch embeddings alongside learned query embeddings. This suggests that "one size fits all" interfaces may not be optimal. As the line between the visual encoder and the language model blurs, exemplified by <Downscaling Intelligence>'s proposal for visual extraction tuning, the modality interface is effectively becoming part of the reasoning engine itself, rather than a mere data conduit.

In conclusion, the modality interface has moved from a trivial linear layer to a core architectural component defining the model's reasoning capabilities. This evolution directly addresses the 'modality gap' introduced in the first paragraph. Architectures like <VisionLLM> and token-based methods attempt to solve the distribution discrepancy by unifying visual and linguistic inputs into a shared generative space, effectively eliminating the gap by treating vision as a 'foreign language.' Conversely, deep fusion mechanisms like <FIBER> tackle the gap by dynamically integrating visual context throughout the reasoning process, rather than relying on a static pre-compression step. However, as highlighted by the 'one size fits all' limitations discussed in <BLIVA>, the field is increasingly recognizing that a single architectural approach may be insufficient. This trade-off is critical: heavy connectors like Q-Former often incur significant latency and parameter overhead, potentially limiting real-time application, whereas deep fusion strategies may offer superior reasoning capabilities but at a higher computational cost per token. Consequently, a central open research question remains: how to design an interface that optimally balances inference efficiency with the rich, dynamic reasoning required for complex tasks, or whether the future lies in the unified generative spaces proposed by <VisionLLM> and <GIT>.

    #### 1.1 Evolution of Modality Interfaces

The architectural design of the modality interface—the bridge that connects visual encoders with Large Language Models (LLMs)—has evolved into a central battleground for defining the capabilities of Multimodal Large Language Models (MLLMs) <MME: A Comprehensive Evaluation Benchmark for Multimodal Large Language Models>. While early efforts, such as <Visual Instruction Tuning>, demonstrated the viability of connecting a visual encoder to an LLM using a simple linear projection layer, the field has rapidly shifted toward more sophisticated architectures. These newer designs aim to handle the "modality gap"—the significant discrepancy in feature distributions and semantic granularity between visual and linguistic modalities <MME: A Comprehensive Evaluation Benchmark for Multimodal Large Language Models>—more effectively, resulting in a spectrum of interfaces ranging from specialized compression modules to deep, interleaved fusion strategies. This evolution reflects a broader taxonomy of projection architectures, generally categorized into: (1) Linear/Projector-based approaches, (2) Q-Former based learnable query architectures, (3) Cross-Attention based fusion mechanisms, and (4) Token-based or direct injection methods <2306.13549>. This section first examines connector-based approaches, then discusses deep fusion strategies, and finally analyzes the trend toward architectural unification.

This architectural progression is not merely a design choice but is deeply intertwined with the scaling laws and data efficiency of MLLMs. As highlighted in recent surveys <2306.13549>, the primary goal of these interfaces is to align the representation spaces of vision and language without disrupting the rich semantic knowledge embedded within the frozen LLM. The choice of interface dictates the model's ability to process high-resolution inputs, handle multiple images, and perform complex reasoning. For instance, while linear projectors offer minimal latency, they often struggle with the semantic misalignment, whereas more complex connectors like Q-Former introduce significant parameter overhead but enable better feature compression. The tension between efficiency and expressive power remains a defining characteristic of this field, driving research toward hybrid approaches that seek the best of both worlds.

#### 1.1.1 Linear and Simple Projector Architectures

The foundational approach to bridging the modality gap relies on simple linear projection layers. <Visual Instruction Tuning> first demonstrated the viability of this method by connecting a visual encoder to an LLM using a single linear layer, establishing a baseline for parameter efficiency. This approach aligns visual features into the language embedding space with minimal overhead, though it often struggles with the significant distributional discrepancy between modalities. <LLaVA> further validated this paradigm, showing that such simple projectors, when combined with high-quality instruction tuning data, can yield impressive multimodal capabilities. While highly efficient, these linear methods typically lack the capacity to compress or restructure visual information deeply, often leading to suboptimal performance on complex tasks requiring fine-grained visual understanding.

#### 1.1.2 Connector-Based Architectures

To address the limitations of simple projectors, a prominent approach involves the use of specialized modules to map visual tokens into the semantic space of the LLM. <MiniGPT-4> utilizes Q-Former (inspired by BLIP-2) to learn a set of learnable queries (typically 32) that aggregate visual features from a frozen ViT before they are fed into the LLM. This method proved that treating visual input as a sequence of compressed, highly relevant features allows an otherwise frozen LLM to understand complex modalities, unlocking emergent capabilities like website generation from sketches. Similarly, <KOSMOS-1> adopts a Perceiver-like architecture to handle interleaved multimodal inputs within a single Transformer. This architecture utilizes a cross-attention mechanism to iteratively compress input sequences into a fixed set of latent tokens, allowing it to process variable-length multimodal data efficiently. By employing a mapping network and special boundary tokens, it effectively unifies modalities. However, reliance on complex, pre-trained feature compressors introduces significant parameter overhead and latency. For instance, <BLIVA> integrates Q-Former-style embeddings with patch embeddings to better handle text-rich images, but this design adds computational cost. This trade-off between compression efficiency and computational cost defines a key tension in early connector designs.

The mechanism of Q-Former in <MiniGPT-4> is particularly noteworthy for its role as a feature bottleneck. By freezing the visual encoder and training only the query vectors and the projection layer, the model learns to extract the most relevant visual information conditioned on the learnable queries. This process effectively reduces the sequence length of visual tokens (e.g., from thousands of patch features to 32 queries), which significantly lowers the computational burden on the subsequent LLM. However, this aggressive compression can lead to information loss, especially for tasks requiring fine-grained spatial understanding. In contrast, the Perceiver architecture in <KOSMOS-1> uses a recurrent cross-attention mechanism that iteratively refines a set of latent tokens against the full visual input. This allows it to maintain a richer representation of the visual scene compared to the single-pass aggregation of Q-Former, albeit at the cost of increased inference time due to the iterative process.

Comparative analysis reveals distinct performance trade-offs. While Q-Former-based connectors like <MiniGPT-4> offer lower latency and parameter counts, they may struggle with complex visual reasoning that requires detailed spatial awareness. The Perceiver-based approach in <KOSMOS-1> provides better handling of variable-length inputs and potentially richer feature integration, but introduces higher computational costs. <BLIVA> attempts to mitigate the information loss of pure Q-Former approaches by retaining raw patch embeddings alongside the compressed queries, thereby preserving high-resolution visual details for text-rich images. This hybrid approach highlights an ongoing design tension: balancing the need for efficient, compressed representations that fit within LLM context windows against the necessity of preserving sufficient visual fidelity for complex reasoning tasks.

#### 1.1.3 Deep Fusion Mechanisms

In reaction to the complexity of specialized connectors, recent research has sought to integrate vision and language more deeply within the model backbone. <mPLUG-Owl2> introduces a "modality-adaptive module" that preserves modality-specific features, arguing that strict fusion can degrade text-only capabilities. It decouples the visual pathway while still sharing a universal language decoder. Conversely, <FIBER> argues for "Fusion in the Backbone." Instead of a separate connector, it interleaves cross-attention layers directly into the LLM’s encoder and decoder context. This deep fusion allows the model to reason about visual dependencies dynamically throughout the generation process, rather than relying on a static pre-fusion step. This trade-off—between specialized, compressed encoders (<MiniGPT-4>) and deep, integrated fusion (<FIBER>)—defines the current tension in interface design.

#### 1.1.4 Token-based and Direct Injection Methods

Distinct from fusion strategies that mix modalities, token-based or direct injection methods treat visual information as a sequence of tokens directly compatible with the LLM's processing pipeline. This approach often involves mapping visual patches to a discrete codebook or generating visual tokens via a specialized tokenizer, allowing the model to process visual inputs much like text. <EMU> exemplifies this by utilizing a visual tokenizer to convert images into discrete tokens, which are then interleaved with text tokens in a unified sequence for generative modeling. Similarly, early pioneering works like <Flamingo> introduced Perceiver resamplers to aggregate visual features into a fixed number of visual tokens, which are then injected into the language model via cross-attention layers. These methods effectively treat vision as a "foreign language" that the LLM can natively understand, moving beyond simple projection to a unified token space. This strategy simplifies the architecture by removing the need for complex, modality-specific connectors, aligning with the trend toward architectural unification.

However, the efficacy of these architectural designs is intrinsically linked to the training data they require. For instance, the "Spatial Vision Aggregator (SVA)" proposed in <Cambrian-1> is a sophisticated connector that relies on a massive, high-quality dataset to fine-tune the interaction between visual backbones and the LLM. SVA typically functions by employing spatial pooling or attention mechanisms to aggregate high-resolution visual features into a compact set of tokens suitable for the LLM. This illustrates a broader trend: as architectures evolve from simple linear layers to complex, deep fusion mechanisms, they often demand more specialized and extensive training data to unlock their full potential, a challenge highlighted by studies on data scaling like <Static Augmentation>.

Beyond the internal fusion strategies discussed above, the industry is also trending toward architectures that prioritize "visual grounding"—explicitly linking text to visual regions. <KOSMOS-2> introduced grounding capabilities by training on grounded image-text pairs, allowing the model to output <region> tags corresponding to bounding boxes. While not strictly an architectural connector change, it alters the output interface to enforce alignment between the visual and textual streams. Similarly, <UniTAB> Unifies Text and Box outputs, proposing a shared token sequence that allows for grounded captioning. The inclusion of an <obj> token represents a structural change to the interface, enabling the model to explicitly align generated words with visual locations.

The trajectory of research suggests that while early interfaces focused on "how to connect" (via linear layers or projectors), the current focus is on "how to integrate." <mPLUG-Owl2> demonstrates that preserving modality-specific features is crucial for general intelligence, while <FIBER> suggests that dynamic fusion is key for fine-grained tasks. As we look at the evolution from <Visual Instruction Tuning>'s linear layer to <VisionLLM>'s generative decoder, the interface is transforming from a static bridge into a dynamic, intelligent router that determines which modality to prioritize and how to fuse context effectively.

The limitations of current interfaces are also becoming apparent in specific domains. For example, <BLIVA> addresses the failure of generalist connectors in text-rich images by explicitly injecting patch embeddings alongside learned query embeddings. This suggests that "one size fits all" interfaces may not be optimal. As the line between the visual encoder and the language model blurs, exemplified by <Downscaling Intelligence>'s proposal for visual extraction tuning, the modality interface is effectively becoming part of the reasoning engine itself, rather than a mere data conduit.

In conclusion, the modality interface has evolved from a trivial linear layer to a core architectural component defining the model's reasoning capabilities. This evolution directly addresses the 'modality gap' introduced in the first paragraph. Architectures like <VisionLLM> and token-based methods attempt to solve the distribution discrepancy by unifying visual and linguistic inputs into a shared generative space, effectively eliminating the gap by treating vision as a 'foreign language.' Conversely, deep fusion mechanisms like <FIBER> tackle the gap by dynamically integrating visual context throughout the reasoning process, rather than relying on a static pre-compression step. This trade-off is critical: heavy connectors like Q-Former often incur significant latency and parameter overhead, potentially limiting real-time application, whereas deep fusion strategies may offer superior reasoning capabilities but at a higher computational cost per token.

Beyond these architectural trade-offs, the field is increasingly recognizing that a single approach may be insufficient for all scenarios. As highlighted by the 'one size fits all' limitations in <BLIVA>, domain-specific challenges—such as processing text-rich images—require specialized adaptations, like retaining raw patch embeddings alongside compressed queries. Furthermore, the interface is evolving to support explicit visual grounding, as seen in <KOSMOS-2>, which alters the output interface to align text with visual regions. Consequently, a central open research question remains: how to design an interface that optimally balances inference efficiency with the rich, dynamic reasoning required for complex tasks, or whether the future lies in the unified generative spaces proposed by <VisionLLM> and <GIT>.

Specifically, <mPLUG-Owl2> utilizes a "Modality-Adaptive Gating" mechanism within its cross-attention layers, allowing the model to selectively attend to visual or textual inputs depending on the current token, thereby maintaining modality integrity. In contrast, <FIBER> replaces standard self-attention layers in the LLM backbone with cross-attention layers that query visual tokens at every layer. This allows visual information to influence the generation of every token, not just the initial context, enabling a more dynamic and fine-grained interaction where visual reasoning is interleaved with linguistic generation.

The trajectory of research suggests that while early interfaces focused on "how to connect" (via linear layers or projectors), the current focus is on "how to integrate." <mPLUG-Owl2> demonstrates that preserving modality-specific features is crucial for general intelligence, while <FIBER> suggests that dynamic fusion is key for fine-grained tasks. The limitations of current interfaces are also becoming apparent in specific domains. For example, <BLIVA> addresses the failure of generalist connectors in text-rich images by explicitly injecting patch embeddings alongside learned query embeddings. This suggests that "one size fits all" interfaces may not be optimal. As the line between the visual encoder and the language model blurs, exemplified by <Downscaling Intelligence>'s proposal for visual extraction tuning, the modality interface is effectively becoming part of the reasoning engine itself, rather than a mere data conduit.

In conclusion, the modality interface has evolved from a trivial linear layer to a core architectural component defining the model's reasoning capabilities. This evolution directly addresses the 'modality gap' introduced in the first paragraph. Architectures like <VisionLLM> and token-based methods attempt to solve the distribution discrepancy by unifying visual and linguistic inputs into a shared generative space, effectively eliminating the gap by treating vision as a 'foreign language.' Conversely, deep fusion mechanisms like <FIBER> tackle the gap by dynamically integrating visual context throughout the reasoning process, rather than relying on a static pre-compression step. This trade-off is critical: heavy connectors like Q-Former often incur significant latency and parameter overhead, potentially limiting real-time application, whereas deep fusion strategies may offer superior reasoning capabilities but at a higher computational cost per token.

Beyond these architectural trade-offs, the field is increasingly recognizing that a single approach may be insufficient for all scenarios. As highlighted by the 'one size fits all' limitations in <BLIVA>, domain-specific challenges—such as processing text-rich images—require specialized adaptations, like retaining raw patch embeddings alongside compressed queries. Furthermore, the interface is evolving to support explicit visual grounding, as seen in <KOSMOS-2>, which alters the output interface to align text with visual regions. Consequently, a central open research question remains: how to design an interface that optimally balances inference efficiency with the rich, dynamic reasoning required for complex tasks, or whether the future lies in the unified generative spaces proposed by <VisionLLM> and <EMU>.

    #### 1.1 Evolution of Modality Interfaces

The architectural design of the modality interface—the bridge that connects visual encoders with Large Language Models (LLMs)—has evolved into a central battleground for defining the capabilities of Multimodal Large Language Models (MLLMs) <2306.13549>. While early efforts, such as <Visual Instruction Tuning>, demonstrated the viability of connecting a visual encoder to an LLM using a simple linear projection layer, the field has rapidly shifted toward more sophisticated architectures. These newer designs aim to handle the "modality gap"—the significant discrepancy in feature distributions and semantic granularity between visual and linguistic modalities <2306.13549>—more effectively, resulting in a spectrum of interfaces ranging from specialized compression modules to deep, interleaved fusion strategies. The modality gap refers to the difference in feature distribution and semantic granularity between visual and linguistic representations. This evolution reflects a broader taxonomy of projection architectures, generally categorized into: (1) Linear/Projector-based approaches, (2) Q-Former based learnable query architectures, (3) Cross-Attention based fusion mechanisms, and (4) Token-based or direct injection methods <2306.13549>. This section first examines connector-based approaches, then discusses deep fusion strategies, and finally analyzes the trend toward architectural unification.

This architectural progression is not merely a design choice but is deeply intertwined with the scaling laws and data efficiency of MLLMs. As highlighted in recent surveys <2306.13549>, the primary goal of these interfaces is to align the representation spaces of vision and language without disrupting the rich semantic knowledge embedded within the frozen LLM. The choice of interface dictates the model's ability to process high-resolution inputs, handle multiple images, and perform complex reasoning. For instance, while linear projectors offer minimal latency, they often struggle with the semantic misalignment, whereas more complex connectors like Q-Former introduce significant parameter overhead but enable better feature compression. The tension between efficiency and expressive power remains a defining characteristic of this field, driving research toward hybrid approaches that seek the best of both worlds.

#### 1.1.1 Linear and Simple Projector Architectures

The foundational approach to bridging the modality gap relies on simple linear projection layers. <Visual Instruction Tuning> first demonstrated the viability of this method by connecting a visual encoder to an LLM using a single linear layer, establishing a baseline for parameter efficiency. This approach aligns visual features into the language embedding space with minimal overhead <Visual Instruction Tuning>, though it often struggles with the significant distributional discrepancy between modalities. <LLaVA> further validated this paradigm, showing that such simple projectors, when combined with high-quality instruction tuning data, can yield impressive multimodal capabilities. While highly efficient, these linear methods typically lack the capacity to compress or restructure visual information deeply, often leading to suboptimal performance on complex tasks requiring fine-grained visual understanding.

#### 1.1.2 Connector-Based Architectures

To address the limitations of simple projectors, a prominent approach involves the use of specialized modules to map visual tokens into the semantic space of the LLM. <MiniGPT-4> utilizes Q-Former (inspired by BLIP-2) to learn a set of learnable queries (typically 32) that aggregate visual features from a frozen ViT before they are fed into the LLM. This method proved that treating visual input as a sequence of compressed, highly relevant features allows an otherwise frozen LLM to understand complex modalities, unlocking emergent capabilities like website generation from sketches. Similarly, <KOSMOS-1> adopts a Perceiver-like architecture to handle interleaved multimodal inputs within a single Transformer. This architecture utilizes a cross-attention mechanism to iteratively compress input sequences into a fixed set of latent tokens. This design allows it to process variable-length multimodal data efficiently. By employing a mapping network and special boundary tokens, it effectively unifies modalities. However, reliance on complex, pre-trained feature compressors introduces significant parameter overhead and latency. For instance, <BLIVA> integrates Q-Former-style embeddings with patch embeddings to better handle text-rich images, but this design adds computational cost. This trade-off between compression efficiency and computational cost defines a key tension in early connector designs.

The mechanism of Q-Former in <MiniGPT-4> is particularly noteworthy for its role as a feature bottleneck. By freezing the visual encoder and training only the query vectors and the projection layer, the model learns to extract the most relevant visual information conditioned on the learnable queries. This process effectively reduces the sequence length of visual tokens (e.g., from thousands of patch features to 32 queries), which significantly lowers the computational burden on the subsequent LLM. However, this aggressive compression can lead to information loss, especially for tasks requiring fine-grained spatial understanding. In contrast, the Perceiver architecture in <KOSMOS-1> uses a recurrent cross-attention mechanism that iteratively refines a set of latent tokens against the full visual input. This allows it to maintain a richer representation of the visual scene compared to the single-pass aggregation of Q-Former, albeit at the cost of increased inference time due to the iterative process.

Comparative analysis reveals distinct performance trade-offs. While Q-Former-based connectors like <MiniGPT-4> offer lower latency and parameter counts, they may struggle with complex visual reasoning that requires detailed spatial awareness. The Perceiver-based approach in <KOSMOS-1> provides better handling of variable-length inputs and potentially richer feature integration, but introduces higher computational costs. <BLIVA> attempts to mitigate the information loss of pure Q-Former approaches by retaining raw patch embeddings alongside the compressed queries, thereby preserving high-resolution visual details for text-rich images. This hybrid approach highlights an ongoing design tension: balancing the need for efficient, compressed representations that fit within LLM context windows against the necessity of preserving sufficient visual fidelity for complex reasoning tasks.

#### 1.1.3 Deep Fusion Mechanisms

In reaction to the complexity of specialized connectors, recent research has sought to integrate vision and language more deeply within the model backbone. <mPLUG-Owl2> introduces a "modality-adaptive module" that preserves modality-specific features, arguing that strict fusion can degrade text-only capabilities. It decouples the visual pathway while still sharing a universal language decoder. Conversely, <FIBER> argues for "Fusion in the Backbone." Instead of a separate connector, it interleaves cross-attention layers directly into the LLM’s encoder and decoder context. This deep fusion allows the model to reason about visual dependencies dynamically throughout the generation process, rather than relying on a static pre-fusion step. This trade-off—between specialized, compressed encoders (<MiniGPT-4>) and deep, integrated fusion (<FIBER>)—defines the current tension in interface design. Specifically, <mPLUG-Owl2> utilizes a "Modality-Adaptive Gating" mechanism within its cross-attention layers, allowing the model to selectively attend to visual or textual inputs depending on the current token, thereby maintaining modality integrity. In contrast, <FIBER> replaces standard self-attention layers in the LLM backbone with cross-attention layers that query visual tokens at every layer. This allows visual information to influence the generation of every token, not just the initial context, enabling a more dynamic and fine-grained interaction where visual reasoning is interleaved with linguistic generation.

#### 1.1.4 Token-based and Direct Injection Methods

While deep fusion mechanisms integrate modalities within the backbone, another line of research moves towards unifying the input space itself by treating visual data as discrete tokens. Distinct from fusion strategies that mix modalities, token-based or direct injection methods treat visual information as a sequence of tokens directly compatible with the LLM's processing pipeline. This approach often involves mapping visual patches to a discrete codebook or generating visual tokens via a specialized tokenizer, allowing the model to process visual inputs much like text. <EMU> exemplifies this by utilizing a visual tokenizer to convert images into discrete tokens, which are then interleaved with text tokens in a unified sequence for generative modeling. Similarly, early pioneering works like <Flamingo> introduced Perceiver resamplers to aggregate visual features into a fixed number of visual tokens, which are then injected into the language model via cross-attention layers. These methods effectively treat vision as a "foreign language" that the LLM can natively understand, moving beyond simple projection to a unified token space. This strategy simplifies the architecture by removing the need for complex, modality-specific connectors, aligning with the trend toward architectural unification. A notable evolution of this concept is found in <VisionLLM>, which proposes treating visual data as a "foreign language" via a generative decoder, allowing the LLM to process visual tokens using its native attention mechanisms. This contrasts with <EMU>'s focus on interleaved generation, as <VisionLLM> emphasizes the flexibility of handling diverse visual tasks within a unified generative framework, further blurring the line between visual encoding and language processing.

However, the efficacy of these architectural designs is intrinsically linked to the training data they require. This illustrates a broader trend: as architectures evolve from simple linear layers to complex, deep fusion mechanisms, they often demand more specialized and extensive training data to unlock their full potential. For instance, simple linear projectors like those in <LLaVA> can often achieve strong performance with pure instruction-tuning data, whereas connector-based architectures like <MiniGPT-4> typically benefit from a two-stage training pipeline involving large-scale pre-training on image-text pairs to learn the mapping between modalities before instruction tuning. Deep fusion models may require even more diverse and complex datasets to effectively train the interleaved attention mechanisms.

The trajectory of research suggests that while early interfaces focused on "how to connect" (via linear layers or projectors), the current focus is on "how to integrate." <mPLUG-Owl2> demonstrates that preserving modality-specific features is crucial for general intelligence, while <FIBER> suggests that dynamic fusion is key for fine-grained tasks. As we look at the evolution from <Visual Instruction Tuning>'s linear layer to <VisionLLM>'s generative decoder, the interface is transforming from a static bridge into a dynamic, intelligent router that determines which modality to prioritize and how to fuse context effectively.

The limitations of current interfaces are also becoming apparent in specific domains. For example, <BLIVA> addresses the failure of generalist connectors in text-rich images by explicitly injecting patch embeddings alongside learned query embeddings. This suggests that "one size fits all" interfaces may not be optimal. As the line between the visual encoder and the language model blurs, exemplified by <Downscaling Intelligence>'s proposal for visual extraction tuning, the modality interface is effectively becoming part of the reasoning engine itself, rather than a mere data conduit.

In conclusion, the modality interface has evolved from a trivial linear layer to a core architectural component defining the model's reasoning capabilities. This evolution directly addresses the 'modality gap' introduced in the first paragraph. Architectures like <VisionLLM> and token-based methods attempt to solve the distribution discrepancy by unifying visual and linguistic inputs into a shared generative space, effectively eliminating the gap by treating vision as a 'foreign language.' Conversely, deep fusion mechanisms like <FIBER> tackle the gap by dynamically integrating visual context throughout the reasoning process, rather than relying on a static pre-compression step. This trade-off is critical: heavy connectors like Q-Former often incur significant latency and parameter overhead, potentially limiting real-time application, whereas deep fusion strategies may offer superior reasoning capabilities but at a higher computational cost per token.

Beyond these architectural trade-offs, the field is increasingly recognizing that a single approach may be insufficient for all scenarios. As highlighted by the 'one size fits all' limitations in <BLIVA>, domain-specific challenges—such as processing text-rich images—require specialized adaptations, like retaining raw patch embeddings alongside compressed queries. Furthermore, the interface is evolving to support explicit visual grounding, as seen in <KOSMOS-2>, which alters the output interface to align text with visual regions. Consequently, a central open research question remains: how to design an interface that optimally balances inference efficiency with the rich, dynamic reasoning required for complex tasks, or whether the future lies in the unified generative spaces proposed by <VisionLLM> and <EMU>.

#### 1.1.5 Summary and Future Directions

In summary, the evolution of modality interfaces reflects a fundamental trade-off between efficiency and expressive power. Simple linear projectors offer minimal latency but struggle with the semantic misalignment inherent in the modality gap. In contrast, sophisticated connectors like Q-Former and deep fusion mechanisms like those in <FIBER> provide better feature alignment and reasoning capabilities at the cost of increased parameter overhead and computational complexity. A recurring theme is the tension between compressing visual information to fit within LLM context windows versus preserving sufficient visual fidelity for complex reasoning. The trend toward architectural unification, as seen in token-based methods, aims to resolve this by treating vision and language within a shared generative space, thereby eliminating the need for distinct modality bridges.

Looking ahead, several open research questions persist. First, how can we design interfaces that dynamically balance efficiency and performance based on task complexity? Second, can we develop hybrid architectures that combine the efficiency of linear methods with the expressive power of deep fusion without incurring prohibitive costs? Finally, as models scale, the role of the interface in facilitating emergent reasoning capabilities remains a critical area for exploration, particularly in how it can support more robust visual grounding and handle diverse, domain-specific challenges.

Architectures like <VisionLLM> and token-based methods attempt to solve the distribution discrepancy by unifying visual and linguistic inputs into a shared generative space, effectively eliminating the gap by treating vision as a 'foreign language.' Conversely, deep fusion mechanisms like <FIBER> tackle the gap by dynamically integrating visual context throughout the reasoning process, rather than relying on a static pre-compression step. This trade-off is critical: heavy connectors like Q-Former often incur significant latency and parameter overhead, potentially limiting real-time application, whereas deep fusion strategies may offer superior reasoning capabilities but at a higher computational cost per token.

Beyond these architectural trade-offs, the field is increasingly recognizing that a single approach may be insufficient for all scenarios. As highlighted by the 'one size fits all' limitations in <BLIVA>, domain-specific challenges—such as processing text-rich images—require specialized adaptations, like retaining raw patch embeddings alongside compressed queries. Furthermore, the interface is evolving to support explicit visual grounding, as seen in <KOSMOS-2>, which alters the output interface to align text with visual regions. Consequently, a central open research question remains: how to design an interface that optimally balances inference efficiency with the rich, dynamic reasoning required for complex tasks, or whether the future lies in the unified generative spaces proposed by <VisionLLM> and <EMU>.

#### 1.1.5 Summary and Future Directions

In summary, the evolution of modality interfaces reflects a fundamental trade-off between efficiency and expressive power. Simple linear projectors offer minimal latency but struggle with the semantic misalignment inherent in the modality gap. In contrast, sophisticated connectors like Q-Former and deep fusion mechanisms like those in <FIBER> provide better feature alignment and reasoning capabilities at the cost of increased parameter overhead and computational complexity. A recurring theme is the tension between compressing visual information to fit within LLM context windows versus preserving sufficient visual fidelity for complex reasoning. The trend toward architectural unification, as seen in token-based methods, aims to resolve this by treating vision and language within a shared generative space, thereby eliminating the need for distinct modality bridges.

Looking ahead, several open research questions persist. First, how can we design interfaces that dynamically balance efficiency and performance based on task complexity? Second, can we develop hybrid architectures that combine the efficiency of linear methods with the expressive power of deep fusion without incurring prohibitive costs? Finally, as models scale, the role of the interface in facilitating emergent reasoning capabilities remains a critical area for exploration, particularly in how it can support more robust visual grounding and handle diverse, domain-specific challenges.

    ### 2. Evolution of Modality Interfaces

The architectural design of the modality interface—the bridge that connects visual encoders with Large Language Models (LLMs)—has evolved into a central battleground for defining the capabilities of Multimodal Large Language Models (MLLMs) <2306.13549>. While early efforts, such as <Visual Instruction Tuning>, demonstrated the viability of connecting a visual encoder to an LLM using a simple linear projection layer, the field has rapidly shifted toward more sophisticated architectures. These newer designs aim to handle the "modality gap"—the significant discrepancy in feature distributions and semantic granularity between visual and linguistic modalities <2306.13549>—more effectively, resulting in a spectrum of interfaces ranging from specialized compression modules to deep, interleaved fusion strategies. The modality gap refers to the difference in feature distribution and semantic granularity between visual and linguistic representations. This evolution reflects a broader taxonomy of projection architectures, generally categorized into: (1) Linear/Projector-based approaches, (2) Q-Former based learnable query architectures, (3) Cross-Attention based fusion mechanisms, and (4) Token-based or direct injection methods <2306.13549>. This section first examines connector-based approaches, then discusses deep fusion strategies, and finally analyzes the trend toward architectural unification.

This architectural progression is not merely a design choice but is deeply intertwined with the scaling laws and data efficiency of MLLMs. As highlighted in recent surveys <2306.13549>, the primary goal of these interfaces is to align the representation spaces of vision and language without disrupting the rich semantic knowledge embedded within the frozen LLM. The choice of interface dictates the model's ability to process high-resolution inputs, handle multiple images, and perform complex reasoning. For instance, while linear projectors offer minimal latency, they often struggle with the semantic misalignment, whereas more complex connectors like Q-Former introduce significant parameter overhead but enable better feature compression. The tension between efficiency and expressive power remains a defining characteristic of this field, driving research toward hybrid approaches that seek the best of both worlds.

#### 2.1.1 Linear and Simple Projector Architectures

The foundational approach to bridging the modality gap relies on simple linear projection layers. <Visual Instruction Tuning> first demonstrated the viability of this method by connecting a visual encoder to an LLM using a single linear layer, establishing a baseline for parameter efficiency. This approach aligns visual features into the language embedding space with minimal overhead <Visual Instruction Tuning>, though it often struggles with the significant distributional discrepancy between modalities. <LLaVA> further validated this paradigm, showing that such simple projectors, when combined with high-quality instruction tuning data, can yield impressive multimodal capabilities. While highly efficient, these linear methods typically lack the capacity to compress or restructure visual information deeply, often leading to suboptimal performance on complex tasks requiring fine-grained visual understanding.

#### 1.1.2 Connector-Based Architectures

To address the limitations of simple projectors, a prominent approach involves the use of specialized modules to map visual tokens into the semantic space of the LLM. <MiniGPT-4> utilizes Q-Former (inspired by BLIP-2) to learn a set of learnable queries (typically 32) that aggregate visual features from a frozen ViT before they are fed into the LLM. This method proved that treating visual input as a sequence of compressed, highly relevant features allows an otherwise frozen LLM to understand complex modalities, unlocking emergent capabilities like website generation from sketches. Similarly, <KOSMOS-1> adopts a Perceiver-like architecture <Perceiver> to handle interleaved multimodal inputs within a single Transformer. This architecture utilizes a cross-attention mechanism to iteratively compress input sequences into a fixed set of latent tokens. This design allows it to process variable-length multimodal data efficiently. By employing a mapping network and special boundary tokens, it effectively unifies modalities. However, reliance on complex, pre-trained feature compressors introduces significant parameter overhead and latency. For instance, <BLIVA> integrates Q-Former-style embeddings with patch embeddings to better handle text-rich images, but this design adds computational cost. This trade-off between compression efficiency and computational cost defines a key tension in early connector designs.

The mechanism of Q-Former in <MiniGPT-4> is particularly noteworthy for its role as a feature bottleneck. By freezing the visual encoder and training only the query vectors and the projection layer, the model learns to extract the most relevant visual information conditioned on the learnable queries. Internally, Q-Former employs a dual cross-attention mechanism: one set of cross-attention layers interacts the learnable queries with the image features to perform visual compression, while another set interacts the queries with text embeddings to facilitate multimodal fusion. This process effectively reduces the sequence length of visual tokens (e.g., from thousands of patch features to 32 queries), which significantly lowers the computational burden on the subsequent LLM. However, this aggressive compression can lead to information loss, especially for tasks requiring fine-grained spatial understanding.

In contrast, the Perceiver architecture in <KOSMOS-1> uses a recurrent cross-attention mechanism that iteratively refines a set of latent tokens against the full visual input. Unlike the single-pass aggregation of Q-Former, the Perceiver updates its latent representation through multiple layers of cross-attention, allowing it to integrate information from the entire visual context progressively. This allows it to maintain a richer representation of the visual scene, albeit at the cost of increased inference time due to the iterative process.

Comparative analysis reveals distinct performance trade-offs. While Q-Former-based connectors like <MiniGPT-4> offer lower latency and parameter counts, they may struggle with complex visual reasoning that requires detailed spatial awareness. The Perceiver-based approach in <KOSMOS-1> provides better handling of variable-length inputs and potentially richer feature integration, but introduces higher computational costs. <BLIVA> attempts to mitigate the information loss of pure Q-Former approaches by retaining raw patch embeddings alongside the compressed queries, thereby preserving high-resolution visual details for text-rich images. This hybrid approach highlights an ongoing design tension: balancing the need for efficient, compressed representations that fit within LLM context windows against the necessity of preserving sufficient visual fidelity for complex reasoning tasks.

#### 1.1.3 Deep Fusion Mechanisms

In reaction to the complexity of specialized connectors, recent research has sought to integrate vision and language more deeply within the model backbone. <mPLUG-Owl2> introduces a "modality-adaptive module" that preserves modality-specific features, arguing that strict fusion can degrade text-only capabilities. It decouples the visual pathway while still sharing a universal language decoder. Conversely, <FIBER> argues for "Fusion in the Backbone." Instead of a separate connector, it interleaves cross-attention layers directly into the LLM’s encoder and decoder context. This deep fusion allows the model to reason about visual dependencies dynamically throughout the generation process, rather than relying on a static pre-fusion step. This trade-off—between specialized, compressed encoders (<MiniGPT-4>) and deep, integrated fusion (<FIBER>)—defines the current tension in interface design. Specifically, <mPLUG-Owl2> utilizes a "Modality-Adaptive Gating" mechanism within its cross-attention layers, allowing the model to selectively attend to visual or textual inputs depending on the current token, thereby maintaining modality integrity. In contrast, <FIBER> replaces standard self-attention layers in the LLM backbone with cross-attention layers that query visual tokens at every layer. This allows visual information to influence the generation of every token, not just the initial context, enabling a more dynamic and fine-grained interaction where visual reasoning is interleaved with linguistic generation.

#### 1.1.4 Token-based and Direct Injection Methods

While deep fusion focuses on integrating modalities within the model's processing layers, another distinct paradigm seeks to unify the input space itself by treating visual data as a sequence of tokens. This trend can be broadly categorized into two approaches: discrete tokenization and unified input spaces. These methods treat visual information as a sequence of tokens directly compatible with the LLM's processing pipeline, moving beyond simple projection to a unified token space.

##### Discrete Tokenization

This approach involves mapping visual patches to a discrete codebook or generating visual tokens via a specialized tokenizer, allowing the model to process visual inputs much like text. <EMU> exemplifies this by utilizing a visual tokenizer to convert images into discrete tokens, which are then interleaved with text tokens in a unified sequence for generative modeling. Similarly, early pioneering works like <Flamingo> introduced Perceiver resamplers to aggregate visual features into a fixed number of visual tokens, which are then injected into the language model via cross-attention layers. These methods effectively treat vision as a "foreign language" that the LLM can natively understand.

##### Unified Input Spaces

A notable evolution of this concept is found in <VisionLLM>, which proposes treating visual data as a "foreign language" via a generative decoder, allowing the LLM to process visual tokens using its native attention mechanisms. This contrasts with <EMU>'s focus on interleaved generation of text and image sequences. Specifically, while <EMU> uses discrete tokenization to enable the generation of images and text within a single, interleaved sequence, <VisionLLM> focuses on a unified generative framework for handling diverse visual tasks (e.g., detection, segmentation) by translating them into a language-like format. This strategy simplifies the architecture by removing the need for complex, modality-specific connectors, aligning with the trend toward architectural unification.

However, the efficacy of these architectural designs is intrinsically linked to the training data they require. This illustrates a broader trend: as architectures evolve from simple linear layers to complex, deep fusion mechanisms, they often demand more specialized and extensive training data to unlock their full potential. For instance, simple linear projectors like those in <LLaVA> can often achieve strong performance with pure instruction-tuning data, whereas connector-based architectures like <MiniGPT-4> typically benefit from a two-stage training pipeline involving large-scale pre-training on image-text pairs to learn the mapping between modalities before instruction tuning. Deep fusion models may require even more diverse and complex datasets to effectively train the interleaved attention mechanisms.

The trajectory of research suggests that while early interfaces focused on "how to connect" (via linear layers or projectors), the current focus is on "how to integrate." <mPLUG-Owl2> demonstrates that preserving modality-specific features is crucial for general intelligence, while <FIBER> suggests that dynamic fusion is key for fine-grained tasks. As we look at the evolution from <Visual Instruction Tuning>'s linear layer to <VisionLLM>'s generative decoder, the interface is transforming from a static bridge into a dynamic, intelligent router that determines which modality to prioritize and how to fuse context effectively.

The limitations of current interfaces are also becoming apparent in specific domains. For example, <BLIVA> addresses the failure of generalist connectors in text-rich images by explicitly injecting patch embeddings alongside learned query embeddings. This suggests that "one size fits all" interfaces may not be optimal. As the line between the visual encoder and the language model blurs, exemplified by <Downscaling Intelligence>'s proposal for visual extraction tuning, the modality interface is effectively becoming part of the reasoning engine itself, rather than a mere data conduit.

In conclusion, the modality interface has evolved from a trivial linear layer to a core architectural component defining the model's reasoning capabilities. This evolution directly addresses the 'modality gap' introduced in the first paragraph. Architectures like <VisionLLM> and token-based methods attempt to solve the distribution discrepancy by unifying visual and linguistic inputs into a shared generative space, effectively eliminating the gap by treating vision as a 'foreign language.' Conversely, deep fusion mechanisms like <FIBER> tackle the gap by dynamically integrating visual context throughout the reasoning process, rather than relying on a static pre-compression step. This trade-off is critical: heavy connectors like Q-Former often incur significant latency and parameter overhead, potentially limiting real-time application, whereas deep fusion strategies may offer superior reasoning capabilities but at a higher computational cost per token.

Beyond these architectural trade-offs, the field is increasingly recognizing that a single approach may be insufficient for all scenarios. As highlighted by the 'one size fits all' limitations in <BLIVA>, domain-specific challenges—such as processing text-rich images—require specialized adaptations, like retaining raw patch embeddings alongside compressed queries. Furthermore, the interface is evolving to support explicit visual grounding, as seen in <KOSMOS-2>, which alters the output interface to align text with visual regions. Consequently, a central open research question remains: how to design an interface that optimally balances inference efficiency with the rich, dynamic reasoning required for complex tasks, or whether the future lies in the unified generative spaces proposed by <VisionLLM> and <EMU>.

#### 1.1.5 Summary and Future Directions

In summary, the evolution of modality interfaces reflects a fundamental trade-off between efficiency and expressive power. Simple linear projectors offer minimal latency but struggle with the semantic misalignment inherent in the modality gap. In contrast, sophisticated connectors like Q-Former and deep fusion mechanisms like those in <FIBER> provide better feature alignment and reasoning capabilities at the cost of increased parameter overhead and computational complexity. A recurring theme is the tension between compressing visual information to fit within LLM context windows versus preserving sufficient visual fidelity for complex reasoning. The trend toward architectural unification, as seen in token-based methods, aims to resolve this by treating vision and language within a shared generative space, thereby eliminating the need for distinct modality bridges.

Looking ahead, a critical research direction is investigating whether 'dynamic sparsity' in fusion layers could resolve the efficiency/expressiveness tension. This hypothesis suggests that models could learn to selectively activate only the most relevant cross-modal connections for a given task or input, thereby achieving the expressiveness of deep fusion mechanisms like <FIBER> while maintaining the efficiency profile of simpler architectures. Such an approach could dynamically balance performance and computational cost, offering a potential path toward interfaces that are both highly capable and practical for real-time applications.
