Specifically, <EMU> utilizes a visual tokenizer to convert images into discrete tokens, which are then interleaved with text tokens in a unified sequence for generative modeling. In contrast, <VisionLLM> focuses on a unified generative framework for handling diverse visual tasks (e.g., detection, segmentation) by translating them into a language-like format. This strategy simplifies the architecture by removing the need for complex, modality-specific connectors, aligning with the trend toward architectural unification.

However, the efficacy of these architectural designs is intrinsically linked to the training data they require. This illustrates a broader trend: as architectures evolve from simple linear layers to complex, deep fusion mechanisms, they often demand more specialized and extensive training data to unlock their full potential. For instance, simple linear projectors like those in <LLaVA> can often achieve strong performance with pure instruction-tuning data, whereas connector-based architectures like <MiniGPT-4> typically benefit from a two-stage training pipeline involving large-scale pre-training on image-text pairs to learn the mapping between modalities before instruction tuning. Deep fusion models may require even more diverse and complex datasets to effectively train the interleaved attention mechanisms.

The trajectory of research suggests that while early interfaces focused on "how to connect" (via linear layers or projectors), the current focus is on "how to integrate." <mPLUG-Owl2> demonstrates that preserving modality-specific features is crucial for general intelligence, while <FIBER> suggests that dynamic fusion is key for fine-grained tasks. As we look at the evolution from <Visual Instruction Tuning>'s linear layer to <VisionLLM>'s generative decoder, the interface is transforming from a static bridge into a dynamic, intelligent router that determines which modality to prioritize and how to fuse context effectively.

The limitations of current interfaces are also becoming apparent in specific domains. For example, <BLIVA> addresses the failure of generalist connectors in text-rich images by explicitly injecting patch embeddings alongside learned query embeddings. This suggests that "one size fits all" interfaces may not be optimal. As the line between the visual encoder and the language model blurs, exemplified by <Downscaling Intelligence>'s proposal for visual extraction tuning, the modality interface is effectively becoming part of the reasoning engine itself, rather than a mere data conduit.

In conclusion, the modality interface has evolved from a trivial linear layer to a core architectural component defining the model's reasoning capabilities. This evolution directly addresses the 'modality gap' introduced in the first paragraph. Architectures like <VisionLLM> and token-based methods attempt to solve the distribution discrepancy by unifying visual and linguistic inputs into a shared generative space, effectively eliminating the gap by treating vision as a 'foreign language.' Conversely, deep fusion mechanisms like <FIBER> tackle the gap by dynamically integrating visual context throughout the reasoning process, rather than relying on a static pre-compression step. This trade-off is critical: heavy connectors like Q-Former often incur significant latency and parameter overhead, potentially limiting real-time application, whereas deep fusion strategies may offer superior reasoning capabilities but at a higher computational cost per token.

Beyond these architectural trade-offs, the field is increasingly recognizing that a single approach may be insufficient for all scenarios. As highlighted by the 'one size fits all' limitations in <BLIVA>, domain-specific challenges—such as processing text-rich images—require specialized adaptations, like retaining raw patch embeddings alongside compressed queries. Furthermore, the interface is evolving to support explicit visual grounding, as seen in <KOSMOS-2>, which alters the output interface to align text with visual regions. Consequently, a central open research question remains: how to design an interface that optimally balances inference efficiency with the rich, dynamic reasoning required for complex tasks, or whether the future lies in the unified generative spaces proposed by <VisionLLM> and <EMU>.

#### 1.1.5 Summary and Future Directions

In summary, the evolution of modality interfaces reflects a shift from static, specialized compression (e.g., Q-Former) toward dynamic, integrated fusion (e.g., <FIBER>) and unified generative spaces (e.g., <VisionLLM>). While deep fusion mechanisms offer superior reasoning by interleaving visual context throughout generation, they often incur significant computational overhead per token. Conversely, unified token-based methods eliminate the modality gap by treating vision as a 'foreign language' but may lack the efficiency of dedicated compression modules. A recurring tension remains between compressing visual information to fit within LLM context windows and preserving sufficient fidelity for complex reasoning.

Looking ahead, a critical research direction is investigating whether 'dynamic sparsity' in fusion layers could resolve this tension. This hypothesis suggests that models could learn to selectively activate only the most relevant cross-modal connections for a given task or input, thereby achieving the expressiveness of deep fusion mechanisms like <FIBER> while maintaining the efficiency profile of simpler architectures. Such an approach could dynamically balance performance and computational cost, offering a potential path toward interfaces that are both highly capable and practical for real-time applications.

    ### 2. Evolution of Modality Interfaces

The architectural design of the modality interface—the bridge that connects visual encoders with Large Language Models (LLMs)—has evolved into a central battleground for defining the capabilities of Multimodal Large Language Models (MLLMs) <2306.13549>. While early efforts, such as <Visual Instruction Tuning>, demonstrated the viability of connecting a visual encoder to an LLM using a simple linear projection layer, the field has rapidly shifted toward more sophisticated architectures. These newer designs aim to handle the "modality gap"—the significant discrepancy in feature distributions and semantic granularity between visual and linguistic modalities <2306.13549>—more effectively, resulting in a spectrum of interfaces ranging from specialized compression modules to deep, interleaved fusion strategies. The modality gap refers to the difference in feature distribution and semantic granularity between visual and linguistic representations. This evolution reflects a broader taxonomy of projection architectures, generally categorized into: (1) Linear/Projector-based approaches, (2) Q-Former based learnable query architectures, (3) Cross-Attention based fusion mechanisms, and (4) Token-based or direct injection methods <2306.13549>. This section first examines connector-based approaches, then discusses deep fusion strategies, and finally analyzes the trend toward architectural unification.

This architectural progression is not merely a design choice but is deeply intertwined with the scaling laws and data efficiency of MLLMs. As highlighted in recent surveys <2306.13549>, the primary goal of these interfaces is to align the representation spaces of vision and language without disrupting the rich semantic knowledge embedded within the frozen LLM. For instance, linear projectors often struggle with high-resolution inputs due to the fixed context window of the LLM, whereas token-based methods like <EMU> or deep fusion like <FIBER> are designed to handle variable-length or interleaved inputs more naturally.

This evolution reflects a fundamental tension between efficiency and expressive power. For instance, while linear projectors offer minimal latency, they often struggle with the semantic misalignment inherent in the modality gap. In contrast, more complex connectors like Q-Former introduce significant parameter overhead but enable better feature compression. This trade-off drives research toward hybrid approaches that seek the best of both worlds.

#### 2.1.1 Linear and Simple Projector Architectures

The foundational approach to bridging the modality gap relies on simple linear projection layers. <LLaVA> first demonstrated the viability of this method by connecting a visual encoder to an LLM using a single linear layer, establishing a baseline for parameter efficiency. This approach aligns visual features into the language embedding space with minimal overhead <LLaVA>, though it often struggles with the significant distributional discrepancy between modalities. <LLaVA> further validated this paradigm, showing that such simple projectors, when combined with high-quality instruction tuning data, can yield impressive multimodal capabilities. While highly efficient, these linear methods typically lack the capacity to compress or restructure visual information deeply, often leading to suboptimal performance on complex tasks requiring fine-grained visual understanding.

#### 2.1.2 Connector-Based Architectures

To address the limitations of simple projectors, a prominent approach involves the use of specialized modules to map visual tokens into the semantic space of the LLM. <MiniGPT-4> utilizes Q-Former (inspired by BLIP-2) to learn a set of learnable queries (typically 32) that aggregate visual features from a frozen ViT before they are fed into the LLM. This method proved that treating visual input as a sequence of compressed, highly relevant features allows an otherwise frozen LLM to understand complex modalities, unlocking emergent capabilities like website generation from sketches. Similarly, <KOSMOS-1> adopts a Perceiver-like architecture to handle interleaved multimodal inputs within a single Transformer. This architecture utilizes a cross-attention mechanism to iteratively compress input sequences into a fixed set of latent tokens. This design allows it to process variable-length multimodal data efficiently. By employing a mapping network and special boundary tokens, it effectively unifies modalities. However, reliance on complex, pre-trained feature compressors introduces significant parameter overhead and latency. For instance, <BLIVA> integrates Q-Former-style embeddings with patch embeddings to better handle text-rich images, but this design adds computational cost. This trade-off between compression efficiency and computational cost defines a key tension in early connector designs.

The mechanism of Q-Former in <MiniGPT-4> is particularly noteworthy for its role as a feature bottleneck. By freezing the visual encoder and training only the query vectors and the projection layer, the model learns to extract the most relevant visual information conditioned on the learnable queries. Internally, Q-Former employs a dual cross-attention mechanism: one set of cross-attention layers interacts the learnable queries with the image features to perform visual compression, while another set interacts the queries with text embeddings to facilitate multimodal fusion. This process effectively reduces the sequence length of visual tokens (e.g., from thousands of patch features to 32 queries), which significantly lowers the computational burden on the subsequent LLM. However, this aggressive compression can lead to information loss, especially for tasks requiring fine-grained spatial understanding. The foundational training of Q-Former is heavily influenced by the BLIP-2 framework, which utilizes a mixture of three key objectives: Image-Text Contrastive (ITC) loss to align image and text representations in a shared space, ITM loss to fine-grained alignment via binary classification, and Masked Language Modeling (MLM) loss to predict masked text tokens conditioned on visual features. This multi-objective approach trains the queries to distill the most semantically relevant information for language generation. <BLIVA> attempts to mitigate the information loss of pure Q-Former approaches by retaining raw patch embeddings alongside the compressed queries, thereby preserving high-resolution visual details for text-rich images.

In contrast, the Perceiver architecture in <KOSMOS-1> uses a recurrent cross-attention mechanism that iteratively refines a set of latent tokens against the full visual input. Unlike the single-pass aggregation of Q-Former, the Perceiver updates its latent representation through multiple layers of cross-attention, allowing it to integrate information from the entire visual context progressively. This allows it to maintain a richer representation of the visual scene, albeit at the cost of increased inference time due to the iterative process. Perceiver-based methods in architectures like <KOSMOS-1> typically optimize a reconstruction loss (e.g., Mean Squared Error) on the visual features, prioritizing the retention of low-level visual fidelity over semantic alignment with language, which fundamentally shapes their respective compression strategies.

Comparative analysis reveals distinct performance trade-offs. While Q-Former-based connectors like <MiniGPT-4> offer lower latency and parameter counts, they may struggle with complex visual reasoning that requires detailed spatial awareness. This is largely due to their aggressive compression strategy, which reduces the sequence length of visual tokens (e.g., from thousands of patch features to 32 queries), significantly lowering the computational burden on the subsequent LLM but risking information loss. The Perceiver-based approach in <KOSMOS-1> provides better handling of variable-length inputs and potentially richer feature integration, but introduces higher computational costs due to iterative cross-attention layers. This hybrid approach highlights an ongoing design tension: balancing the need for efficient, compressed representations that fit within LLM context windows against the necessity of preserving sufficient visual fidelity for complex reasoning tasks.

#### 2.1.3 Deep Fusion Mechanisms

While the connector-based architectures discussed above rely on static compression to bridge the modality gap, recent research has shifted towards a paradigm of deep fusion. In reaction to the limitations of static pre-fusion, where visual context is fixed before generation begins, this approach seeks to integrate vision and language more deeply within the model backbone. <mPLUG-Owl2> introduces a "modality-adaptive module" that preserves modality-specific features, arguing that strict fusion can degrade text-only capabilities. It decouples the visual pathway while still sharing a universal language decoder. Conversely, <FIBER> argues for "Fusion in the Backbone." Instead of a separate connector, it interleaves cross-attention layers directly into the LLM’s encoder and decoder context. This deep fusion allows the model to reason about visual dependencies dynamically throughout the generation process, rather than relying on a static pre-fusion step. This trade-off—between specialized, compressed encoders (<MiniGPT-4>) and deep, integrated fusion (<FIBER>)—defines the current tension in interface design. Specifically, <mPLUG-Owl2> utilizes a "Modality-Adaptive Gating" mechanism within its cross-attention layers, allowing the model to selectively attend to visual or textual inputs depending on the current token, thereby maintaining modality integrity. In contrast, <FIBER> replaces standard self-attention layers in the LLM backbone with cross-attention layers that query visual tokens at every layer. This allows visual information to influence the generation of every token, not just the initial context, enabling a more dynamic and fine-grained interaction where visual reasoning is interleaved with linguistic generation.

#### 1.1.4 Token-based and Direct Injection Methods

While deep fusion focuses on integrating modalities within the model's processing layers, another distinct paradigm seeks to unify the input space itself by treating visual data as a sequence of tokens. This trend can be broadly categorized into two approaches: discrete tokenization and unified input spaces. These methods treat visual information as a sequence of tokens directly compatible with the LLM's processing pipeline, moving beyond simple projection to a unified token space.

##### Discrete Tokenization

This approach involves mapping visual patches to a discrete codebook or generating visual tokens via a specialized tokenizer, allowing the model to process visual inputs much like text. <EMU> exemplifies this by utilizing a visual tokenizer to convert images into discrete tokens, which are then interleaved with text tokens in a unified sequence for generative modeling. These methods effectively treat vision as a "foreign language" that the LLM can natively understand.

##### Unified Input Spaces

A notable evolution of this concept is found in <VisionLLM>, which proposes treating visual data as a "foreign language" via a generative decoder, allowing the LLM to process visual tokens using its native attention mechanisms. This contrasts with <EMU>'s focus on interleaved generation of text and image sequences. Specifically, <EMU> utilizes a visual tokenizer to convert images into discrete tokens, which are then interleaved with text tokens in a unified sequence for generative modeling. In contrast, <VisionLLM> focuses on a unified generative framework for handling diverse visual tasks (e.g., detection, segmentation) by translating them into a language-like format. This strategy simplifies the architecture by removing the need for complex, modality-specific connectors, aligning with the trend toward architectural unification.

However, the efficacy of these architectural designs is intrinsically linked to the training data they require. This illustrates a broader trend: as architectures evolve from simple linear layers to complex, deep fusion mechanisms, they often demand more specialized and extensive training data to unlock their full potential. For instance, simple linear projectors like those in <LLaVA> can often achieve strong performance with pure instruction-tuning data, whereas connector-based architectures like <MiniGPT-4> typically benefit from a two-stage training pipeline involving large-scale pre-training on image-text pairs to learn the mapping between modalities before instruction tuning. Deep fusion models like <FIBER> require interleaved image-text data during pre-training to effectively learn the cross-attention mechanisms, whereas simpler projectors can rely solely on instruction-tuning data.

The trajectory of research suggests that while early interfaces focused on "how to connect" (via linear layers or projectors), the current focus is on "how to integrate." <mPLUG-Owl2> demonstrates that preserving modality-specific features is crucial for general intelligence, while <FIBER> suggests that dynamic fusion is key for fine-grained tasks. As we look at the evolution from <Visual Instruction Tuning>'s linear layer to <VisionLLM>'s generative decoder, the interface is transforming from a static bridge into a dynamic, intelligent router that determines which modality to prioritize and how to fuse context effectively.

The limitations of current interfaces are also becoming apparent in specific domains. For example, <BLIVA> addresses the failure of generalist connectors in text-rich images by explicitly injecting patch embeddings alongside learned query embeddings. This suggests that "one size fits all" interfaces may not be optimal. As the line between the visual encoder and the language model blurs, exemplified by <Downscaling Intelligence>'s proposal for visual extraction tuning, the modality interface is effectively becoming part of the reasoning engine itself, rather than a mere data conduit.

In conclusion, the modality interface has evolved from a trivial linear layer to a core architectural component defining the model's reasoning capabilities. This evolution directly addresses the 'modality gap' introduced in the first paragraph. Architectures like <VisionLLM> and token-based methods attempt to solve the distribution discrepancy by unifying visual and linguistic inputs into a shared generative space, effectively eliminating the gap by treating vision as a 'foreign language.' Conversely, deep fusion mechanisms like <FIBER> tackle the gap by dynamically integrating visual context throughout the reasoning process, rather than relying on a static pre-compression step. This trade-off is critical: heavy connectors like Q-Former often incur significant latency and parameter overhead, potentially limiting real-time application, whereas deep fusion strategies may offer superior reasoning capabilities but at a higher computational cost per token.

Beyond these architectural trade-offs, the field is increasingly recognizing that a single approach may be insufficient for all scenarios. As highlighted by the 'one size fits all' limitations in <BLIVA>, domain-specific challenges—such as processing text-rich images—require specialized adaptations, like retaining raw patch embeddings alongside compressed queries. Furthermore, the interface is evolving to support explicit visual grounding, as seen in <KOSMOS-2>, which alters the output interface to align text with visual regions. Consequently, a central open research question remains: how to design an interface that optimally balances inference efficiency with the rich, dynamic reasoning required for complex tasks, or whether the future lies in the unified generative spaces proposed by <VisionLLM> and <EMU>.

#### 1.1.5 Summary and Future Directions

In summary, the evolution of modality interfaces reflects a shift from static, specialized compression (e.g., Q-Former) toward dynamic, integrated fusion (e.g., <FIBER>) and unified generative spaces (e.g., <VisionLLM>). While deep fusion mechanisms offer superior reasoning by interleaving visual context throughout generation, they often incur significant computational overhead per token. Conversely, unified token-based methods eliminate the modality gap by treating vision as a 'foreign language' but may lack the efficiency of dedicated compression modules. A recurring tension remains between compressing visual information to fit within LLM context windows and preserving sufficient fidelity for complex reasoning.

Looking ahead, a critical research direction is investigating whether 'dynamic sparsity' in fusion layers could resolve this tension. While this remains largely a hypothesis based on observed trends, preliminary work in related areas, such as the adaptive gating mechanisms in <mPLUG-Owl2>, suggests that selectively prioritizing modalities is a promising path. This hypothesis suggests that models could learn to selectively activate only the most relevant cross-modal connections for a given task or input, thereby achieving the expressiveness of deep fusion mechanisms like <FIBER> while maintaining the efficiency profile of simpler architectures. Such an approach could dynamically balance performance and computational cost, offering a potential path toward interfaces that are both highly capable and practical for real-time applications.

This process effectively reduces the sequence length of visual tokens (e.g., from thousands of patch features to 32 queries), which significantly lowers the computational burden on the subsequent LLM. However, this aggressive compression can lead to information loss, especially for tasks requiring fine-grained spatial understanding. The foundational training of Q-Former is heavily influenced by the BLIP-2 framework, which utilizes a mixture of three key objectives: Image-Text Contrastive (ITC) loss to align image and text representations in a shared space, ITM loss to fine-grained alignment via binary classification, and Masked Language Modeling (MLM) loss to predict masked text tokens conditioned on visual features. This multi-objective approach trains the queries to distill the most semantically relevant information for language generation. <BLIVA> attempts to mitigate the information loss of pure Q-Former approaches by retaining raw patch embeddings alongside the compressed queries, thereby preserving high-resolution visual details for text-rich images.

In contrast, the Perceiver architecture in <KOSMOS-1> uses a recurrent cross-attention mechanism that iteratively refines a set of latent tokens against the full visual input. Unlike the single-pass aggregation of Q-Former, the Perceiver updates its latent representation through multiple layers of cross-attention, allowing it to integrate information from the entire visual context progressively. This allows it to maintain a richer representation of the visual scene, albeit at the cost of increased inference time due to the iterative process. Perceiver-based methods in architectures like <KOSMOS-1> typically optimize a reconstruction loss (e.g., Mean Squared Error) on the visual features, prioritizing the retention of low-level visual fidelity over semantic alignment with language, which fundamentally shapes their respective compression strategies.

Comparative analysis reveals distinct performance trade-offs. While Q-Former-based connectors like <MiniGPT-4> offer lower latency and parameter counts, they may struggle with complex visual reasoning that requires detailed spatial awareness. This is largely due to their aggressive compression strategy, which reduces the sequence length of visual tokens (e.g., from thousands of patch features to 32 queries), significantly lowering the computational burden on the subsequent LLM but risking information loss. The Perceiver-based approach in <KOSMOS-1> provides better handling of variable-length inputs and potentially richer feature integration, but introduces higher computational costs due to iterative cross-attention layers. This hybrid approach highlights an ongoing design tension: balancing the need for efficient, compressed representations that fit within LLM context windows against the necessity of preserving sufficient visual fidelity for complex reasoning tasks.

#### 2.1.3 Deep Fusion Mechanisms

While the connector-based architectures discussed above rely on static compression to bridge the modality gap, recent research has shifted towards a paradigm of deep fusion. In reaction to the limitations of static pre-fusion, where visual context is fixed before generation begins, this approach seeks to integrate vision and language more deeply within the model backbone. <mPLUG-Owl2> introduces a "modality-adaptive module" that preserves modality-specific features, arguing that strict fusion can degrade text-only capabilities. It decouples the visual pathway while still sharing a universal language decoder. Conversely, <FIBER> argues for "Fusion in the Backbone." Instead of a separate connector, it interleaves cross-attention layers directly into the LLM’s encoder and decoder context. This deep fusion allows the model to reason about visual dependencies dynamically throughout the generation process, rather than relying on a static pre-fusion step. This trade-off—between specialized, compressed encoders (<MiniGPT-4>) and deep, integrated fusion (<FIBER>)—defines the current tension in interface design. Specifically, <mPLUG-Owl2> utilizes a "Modality-Adaptive Gating" mechanism within its cross-attention layers, allowing the model to selectively attend to visual or textual inputs depending on the current token, thereby maintaining modality integrity. In contrast, <FIBER> replaces standard self-attention layers in the LLM backbone with cross-attention layers that query visual tokens at every layer. This allows visual information to influence the generation of every token, not just the initial context, enabling a more dynamic and fine-grained interaction where visual reasoning is interleaved with linguistic generation.

#### 2.1.4 Token-based and Direct Injection Methods

While deep fusion focuses on integrating modalities within the model's processing layers, another distinct paradigm seeks to unify the input space itself by treating visual data as a sequence of tokens. This trend can be broadly categorized into two approaches: discrete tokenization and unified input spaces. These methods treat visual information as a sequence of tokens directly compatible with the LLM's processing pipeline, moving beyond simple projection to a unified token space.

##### Discrete Tokenization

This approach involves mapping visual patches to a discrete codebook or generating visual tokens via a specialized tokenizer, allowing the model to process visual inputs much like text. <EMU> exemplifies this by utilizing a visual tokenizer to convert images into discrete tokens, which are then interleaved with text tokens in a unified sequence for generative modeling. These methods effectively treat vision as a "foreign language" that the LLM can natively understand.

##### Unified Input Spaces

A notable evolution of this concept is found in <VisionLLM>, which proposes treating visual data as a "foreign language" via a generative decoder, allowing the LLM to process visual tokens using its native attention mechanisms. This contrasts with <EMU>'s focus on interleaved generation of text and image sequences. Specifically, <EMU> utilizes a visual tokenizer to convert images into discrete tokens, which are then interleaved with text tokens in a unified sequence for generative modeling. In contrast, <VisionLLM> focuses on a unified generative framework for handling diverse visual tasks (e.g., detection, segmentation) by translating them into a language-like format. This strategy simplifies the architecture by removing the need for complex, modality-specific connectors, aligning with the trend toward architectural unification.

However, the efficacy of these architectural designs is intrinsically linked to the training data they require. This illustrates a broader trend: as architectures evolve from simple linear layers to complex, deep fusion mechanisms, they often demand more specialized and extensive training data to unlock their full potential. For instance, simple linear projectors like those in <LLaVA> can often achieve strong performance with pure instruction-tuning data, whereas connector-based architectures like <MiniGPT-4> typically benefit from a two-stage training pipeline involving large-scale pre-training on image-text pairs to learn the mapping between modalities before instruction tuning. Deep fusion models like <FIBER> require interleaved image-text data during pre-training to effectively learn the cross-attention mechanisms, whereas simpler projectors can rely solely on instruction-tuning data.

The trajectory of research suggests that while early interfaces focused on "how to connect" (via linear layers or projectors), the current focus is on "how to integrate." <mPLUG-Owl2> demonstrates that preserving modality-specific features is crucial for general intelligence, while <FIBER> suggests that dynamic fusion is key for fine-grained tasks. As we look at the evolution from <Visual Instruction Tuning>'s linear layer to <VisionLLM>'s generative decoder, the interface is transforming from a static bridge into a dynamic, intelligent router that determines which modality to prioritize and how to fuse context effectively.

The limitations of current interfaces are also becoming apparent in specific domains. For example, <BLIVA> addresses the failure of generalist connectors in text-rich images by explicitly injecting patch embeddings alongside learned query embeddings. This suggests that "one size fits all" interfaces may not be optimal. As the line between the visual encoder and the language model blurs, exemplified by <Downscaling Intelligence>'s proposal for visual extraction tuning, the modality interface is effectively becoming part of the reasoning engine itself, rather than a mere data conduit.

Furthermore, the interface is evolving to support explicit visual grounding, as seen in <KOSMOS-2>, which alters the output interface to align text with visual regions. This shift highlights that the modality interface is not just about feature alignment but also about enabling fine-grained, spatially-aware interaction.

In conclusion, the modality interface has evolved from a trivial linear layer to a core architectural component defining the model's reasoning capabilities. This evolution directly addresses the 'modality gap' introduced in the first paragraph. Architectures like <VisionLLM> and token-based methods attempt to solve the distribution discrepancy by unifying visual and linguistic inputs into a shared generative space, effectively eliminating the gap by treating vision as a 'foreign language.' Conversely, deep fusion mechanisms like <FIBER> tackle the gap by dynamically integrating visual context throughout the reasoning process, rather than relying on a static pre-compression step. This trade-off is critical: heavy connectors like Q-Former often incur significant latency and parameter overhead, potentially limiting real-time application, whereas deep fusion strategies may offer superior reasoning capabilities but at a higher computational cost per token.

Beyond these architectural trade-offs, the field is increasingly recognizing that a single approach may be insufficient for all scenarios. As highlighted by the 'one size fits all' limitations in <BLIVA>, domain-specific challenges—such as processing text-rich images—require specialized adaptations, like retaining raw patch embeddings alongside compressed queries. Consequently, a central open research question remains: how to design an interface that optimally balances inference efficiency with the rich, dynamic reasoning required for complex tasks, or whether the future lies in the unified generative spaces proposed by <VisionLLM> and <EMU>.

#### 1.1.5 Summary and Future Directions

In summary, the evolution of modality interfaces reflects a shift from static, specialized compression (e.g., Q-Former) toward dynamic, integrated fusion (e.g., <FIBER>) and unified generative spaces (e.g., <VisionLLM>). While deep fusion mechanisms offer superior reasoning by interleaving visual context throughout generation, they often incur significant computational overhead per token. Conversely, unified token-based methods eliminate the modality gap by treating vision as a 'foreign language' but may lack the efficiency of dedicated compression modules. A recurring tension remains between compressing visual information to fit within LLM context windows and preserving sufficient fidelity for complex reasoning.

Looking ahead, a critical research direction is investigating whether 'dynamic sparsity' in fusion layers could resolve this tension. While this remains largely a hypothesis based on observed trends, preliminary work in related areas, such as the adaptive gating mechanisms in <mPLUG-Owl2>, suggests that selectively prioritizing modalities is a promising path. Future work may explore whether models can learn to selectively activate only the most relevant cross-modal connections for a given task or input, thereby achieving the expressiveness of deep fusion mechanisms like <FIBER> while maintaining the efficiency profile of simpler architectures. Such an approach could dynamically balance performance and computational cost, offering a potential path toward interfaces that are both highly capable and practical for real-time applications.

    ### 2. Evolution of Modality Interfaces

The architectural design of the modality interface—the bridge that connects visual encoders with Large Language Models (LLMs)—has evolved into a central battleground for defining the capabilities of Multimodal Large Language Models (MLLMs) <2306.13549>. While early efforts, such as <LLaVA>, demonstrated the viability of connecting a visual encoder to an LLM using a simple linear projection layer, the field has rapidly shifted toward more sophisticated architectures. These newer designs aim to handle the "modality gap"—the significant discrepancy in feature distributions and semantic granularity between visual and linguistic modalities <2306.13549>—more effectively. This evolution reflects a broader taxonomy of projection architectures, generally categorized into: (1) Linear/Projector-based approaches, (2) Q-Former based learnable query architectures, (3) Cross-Attention based fusion mechanisms, and (4) Token-based or direct injection methods <2306.13549>. This section first examines connector-based approaches, then discusses deep fusion strategies, and finally analyzes the trend toward architectural unification.

This architectural progression is not merely a design choice but is deeply intertwined with the scaling laws and data efficiency of MLLMs. As highlighted in recent surveys <2306.13549>, the primary goal of these interfaces is to align the representation spaces of vision and language without disrupting the rich semantic knowledge embedded within the frozen LLM. For instance, linear projectors often struggle with high-resolution inputs due to the fixed context window of the LLM, whereas token-based methods like <EMU> or deep fusion like <FIBER> are designed to handle variable-length or interleaved inputs more naturally.

This evolution reflects a fundamental tension between efficiency and expressive power. For instance, while linear projectors offer minimal latency, they often struggle with the semantic misalignment inherent in the modality gap. In contrast, more complex connectors like Q-Former introduce significant parameter overhead but enable better feature compression. This trade-off drives research toward hybrid approaches that seek the best of both worlds.

#### 2.1 Linear Projectors

The foundational approach to bridging the modality gap relies on simple linear projection layers. <LLaVA> first demonstrated the viability of this method by connecting a visual encoder to an LLM using a single linear layer, establishing a baseline for parameter efficiency. This approach aligns visual features into the language embedding space with minimal overhead <LLaVA>, though it often struggles with the significant distributional discrepancy between modalities. <LLaVA> further validated this paradigm, showing that such simple projectors, when combined with high-quality instruction tuning data, can yield impressive multimodal capabilities. While highly efficient, these linear methods typically lack the capacity to compress or restructure visual information deeply, often leading to suboptimal performance on complex tasks requiring fine-grained visual understanding.

#### 2.2 Connector-Based Architectures

To address the limitations of simple projectors, a prominent approach involves the use of specialized modules to map visual tokens into the semantic space of the LLM. <MiniGPT-4> utilizes Q-Former (inspired by BLIP-2) to learn a set of learnable queries (typically 32) that aggregate visual features from a frozen ViT before they are fed into the LLM. This method proved that treating visual input as a sequence of compressed, highly relevant features allows an otherwise frozen LLM to understand complex modalities, unlocking emergent capabilities like website generation from sketches. Similarly, <KOSMOS-1> adopts a Perceiver-like architecture to handle interleaved multimodal inputs within a single Transformer. This architecture utilizes a cross-attention mechanism to iteratively compress input sequences into a fixed set of latent tokens. This design allows it to process variable-length multimodal data efficiently. By employing a mapping network and special boundary tokens, it effectively unifies modalities. However, reliance on complex, pre-trained feature compressors introduces significant parameter overhead and latency. For instance, <BLIVA> integrates Q-Former-style embeddings with patch embeddings to better handle text-rich images, but this design adds computational cost. This trade-off between compression efficiency and computational cost defines a key tension in early connector designs.

The mechanism of Q-Former in <MiniGPT-4> is particularly noteworthy for its role as a feature bottleneck. By freezing the visual encoder and training only the query vectors and the projection layer, the model learns to extract the most relevant visual information conditioned on the learnable queries. Internally, Q-Former employs a dual cross-attention mechanism: one set of cross-attention layers interacts the learnable queries with the image features to perform visual compression, while another set interacts the queries with text embeddings to facilitate multimodal fusion. This process effectively reduces the sequence length of visual tokens (e.g., from thousands of patch features to 32 queries), which significantly lowers the computational burden on the subsequent LLM. However, this aggressive compression can lead to information loss, especially for tasks requiring fine-grained spatial understanding. The foundational training of Q-Former is heavily influenced by the BLIP-2 framework, which utilizes a mixture of three key objectives: Image-Text Contrastive (ITC) loss to align image and text representations in a shared space, ITM loss to fine-grained alignment via binary classification, and Masked Language Modeling (MLM) loss to predict masked text tokens conditioned on visual features. This multi-objective approach trains the queries to distill the most semantically relevant information for language generation. <BLIVA> attempts to mitigate the information loss of pure Q-Former approaches by retaining raw patch embeddings alongside the compressed queries, thereby preserving high-resolution visual details for text-rich images.

In contrast, the Perceiver architecture in <KOSMOS-1> uses a recurrent cross-attention mechanism that iteratively refines a set of latent tokens against the full visual input. Unlike the single-pass aggregation of Q-Former, the Perceiver updates its latent representation through multiple layers of cross-attention, allowing it to integrate information from the entire visual context progressively. This allows it to maintain a richer representation of the visual scene, albeit at the cost of increased inference time due to the iterative process. Perceiver-based methods in architectures like <KOSMOS-1> typically optimize a reconstruction loss (e.g., Mean Squared Error) on the visual features, prioritizing the retention of low-level visual fidelity over semantic alignment with language, which fundamentally shapes their respective compression strategies.

Comparative analysis reveals distinct performance trade-offs. While Q-Former-based connectors like <MiniGPT-4> offer lower latency and parameter counts, they may struggle with complex visual reasoning that requires detailed spatial awareness. This is largely due to their aggressive compression strategy, which reduces the sequence length of visual tokens (e.g., from thousands of patch features to 32 queries), significantly lowering the computational burden on the subsequent LLM but risking information loss. The Perceiver-based approach in <KOSMOS-1> provides better handling of variable-length inputs and potentially richer feature integration, but introduces higher computational costs due to iterative cross-attention layers. This hybrid approach highlights an ongoing design tension: balancing the need for efficient, compressed representations that fit within LLM context windows against the necessity of preserving sufficient visual fidelity for complex reasoning tasks.

#### 2.3 Deep Fusion Mechanisms

While the connector-based architectures discussed above rely on static compression to bridge the modality gap, recent research has shifted towards a paradigm of deep fusion. In reaction to the limitations of static pre-fusion, where visual context is fixed before generation begins, this approach seeks to integrate vision and language more deeply within the model backbone. <mPLUG-Owl2> introduces a "modality-adaptive module" that preserves modality-specific features, arguing that strict fusion can degrade text-only capabilities. It decouples the visual pathway while still sharing a universal language decoder. Conversely, <FIBER> argues for "Fusion in the Backbone." Instead of a separate connector, it interleaves cross-attention layers directly into the LLM’s encoder and decoder context. This deep fusion allows the model to reason about visual dependencies dynamically throughout the generation process, rather than relying on a static pre-fusion step. This trade-off—between specialized, compressed encoders (<MiniGPT-4>) and deep, integrated fusion (<FIBER>)—defines the current tension in interface design. Specifically, <mPLUG-Owl2> utilizes a "Modality-Adaptive Gating" mechanism within its cross-attention layers, allowing the model to selectively attend to visual or textual inputs depending on the current token, thereby maintaining modality integrity. In contrast, <FIBER> replaces standard self-attention layers in the LLM backbone with cross-attention layers that query visual tokens at every layer. This allows visual information to influence the generation of every token, not just the initial context, enabling a more dynamic and fine-grained interaction where visual reasoning is interleaved with linguistic generation.

#### 2.4 Token-Based Methods

While deep fusion focuses on integrating modalities within the model's processing layers, another distinct paradigm seeks to unify the input space itself by treating visual data as a sequence of tokens. This trend can be broadly categorized into two approaches: discrete tokenization and unified input spaces. These methods treat visual information as a sequence of tokens directly compatible with the LLM's processing pipeline, moving beyond simple projection to a unified token space.

##### Discrete Tokenization

This approach focuses on representing images as discrete codes for generative modeling. It involves mapping visual patches to a discrete codebook or generating visual tokens via a specialized tokenizer, allowing the model to process visual inputs much like text. <EMU> exemplifies this by utilizing a visual tokenizer to convert images into discrete tokens, which are then interleaved with text tokens in a unified sequence for generative modeling. These methods effectively treat vision as a "foreign language" that the LLM can natively understand.

##### Unified Input Spaces

This approach focuses on translating visual tasks into a language-like format for task execution. A notable evolution of this concept is found in <VisionLLM>, which proposes treating visual data as a "foreign language" via a generative decoder, allowing the LLM to process visual tokens using its native attention mechanisms. Unlike <EMU>'s focus on interleaved generation of text and image sequences, <VisionLLM> focuses on a unified generative framework for handling diverse visual tasks (e.g., detection, segmentation) by translating them into a language-like format. This strategy simplifies the architecture by removing the need for complex, modality-specific connectors, aligning with the trend toward architectural unification.

However, the efficacy of these architectural designs is intrinsically linked to the training data they require. This illustrates a broader trend: as architectures evolve from simple linear layers to complex, deep fusion mechanisms, they often demand more specialized and extensive training data to unlock their full potential. For instance, simple linear projectors like those in <LLaVA> can often achieve strong performance with pure instruction-tuning data, whereas connector-based architectures like <MiniGPT-4> typically benefit from a two-stage training pipeline involving large-scale pre-training on image-text pairs to learn the mapping between modalities before instruction tuning. Deep fusion models like <FIBER> require interleaved image-text data during pre-training to effectively learn the cross-attention mechanisms, whereas simpler projectors can rely solely on instruction-tuning data.

The trajectory of research suggests that while early interfaces focused on "how to connect" (via linear layers or projectors), the current focus is on "how to integrate." <mPLUG-Owl2> demonstrates that preserving modality-specific features is crucial for general intelligence, while <FIBER> suggests that dynamic fusion is key for fine-grained tasks. As we look at the evolution from <Visual Instruction Tuning>'s linear layer to <VisionLLM>'s generative decoder, the interface is transforming from a static bridge into a dynamic, intelligent router that determines which modality to prioritize and how to fuse context effectively.

The limitations of current interfaces are also becoming apparent in specific domains. For example, <BLIVA> addresses the failure of generalist connectors in text-rich images by explicitly injecting patch embeddings alongside learned query embeddings. This suggests that "one size fits all" interfaces may not be optimal. As the line between the visual encoder and the language model blurs, exemplified by <Downscaling Intelligence>'s proposal for visual extraction tuning, the modality interface is effectively becoming part of the reasoning engine itself, rather than a mere data conduit.

Furthermore, the interface is evolving to support explicit visual grounding, as seen in <KOSMOS-2>, which alters the output interface to align text with visual regions. This shift highlights that the modality interface is not just about feature alignment but also about enabling fine-grained, spatially-aware interaction.

In conclusion, the modality interface has evolved from a trivial linear layer to a core architectural component defining the model's reasoning capabilities. This evolution directly addresses the 'modality gap' introduced in the first paragraph. Architectures like <VisionLLM> and token-based methods attempt to solve the distribution discrepancy by unifying visual and linguistic inputs into a shared generative space, effectively eliminating the gap by treating vision as a 'foreign language.' Conversely, deep fusion mechanisms like <FIBER> tackle the gap by dynamically integrating visual context throughout the reasoning process, rather than relying on a static pre-compression step. This trade-off is critical: heavy connectors like Q-Former often incur significant latency and parameter overhead, potentially limiting real-time application, whereas deep fusion strategies may offer superior reasoning capabilities but at a higher computational cost per token.

Beyond these architectural trade-offs, the field is increasingly recognizing that a single approach may be insufficient for all scenarios. As highlighted by the 'one size fits all' limitations in <BLIVA>, domain-specific challenges—such as processing text-rich images—require specialized adaptations, like retaining raw patch embeddings alongside compressed queries. Furthermore, the efficacy of these interfaces is heavily dependent on the training paradigm. For instance, connector-based models like <MiniGPT-4> often require extensive pre-training on image-text pairs to learn the mapping effectively, whereas token-based methods like <EMU> rely on the joint training of a visual tokenizer and the LLM. Consequently, a central open research question remains: how to design an interface that optimally balances inference efficiency with the rich, dynamic reasoning required for complex tasks, or whether the future lies in the unified generative spaces proposed by <VisionLLM> and <EMU>.

#### 2.5 Summary and Future Directions

In summary, the evolution of modality interfaces reflects a shift from static, specialized compression (e.g., Q-Former) toward dynamic, integrated fusion (e.g., <FIBER>) and unified generative spaces (e.g., <VisionLLM>). While deep fusion mechanisms offer superior reasoning by interleaving visual context throughout generation, they often incur significant computational overhead per token. Conversely, unified token-based methods eliminate the modality gap by treating vision as a 'foreign language' but may lack the efficiency of dedicated compression modules. A recurring tension remains between compressing visual information to fit within LLM context windows and preserving sufficient fidelity for complex reasoning.

Looking ahead, a critical research direction is investigating whether 'dynamic sparsity' in fusion layers could resolve this tension. While this remains largely a hypothesis based on observed trends, preliminary work in related areas, such as the adaptive gating mechanisms in <mPLUG-Owl2>, suggests that selectively prioritizing modalities is a promising path. Future work may explore whether models can learn to selectively activate only the most relevant cross-modal connections for a given task or input, thereby achieving the expressiveness of deep fusion mechanisms like <FIBER> while maintaining the efficiency profile of simpler architectures. Such an approach could dynamically balance performance and computational cost, offering a potential path toward interfaces that are both highly capable and practical for real-time applications.

    ### 2. Evolution of Modality Interfaces

The architectural design of the modality interface—the bridge that connects visual encoders with Large Language Models (LLMs)—has evolved into a central battleground for defining the capabilities of Multimodal Large Language Models (MLLMs) <2306.13549>. This evolution traces a clear trajectory from simple, static bridges to complex, dynamic integration mechanisms. Early efforts, such as <LLaVA>, established the viability of simple linear projection layers, but their inability to bridge the "modality gap"—the significant discrepancy in feature distributions and semantic granularity between visual and linguistic modalities <2306.13549>—drove the development of more sophisticated architectures. This progression can be viewed as a series of evolutionary stages: (1) **Linear Projectors**, the foundational baseline; (2) **Connector-based Architectures** (e.g., Q-Former), which introduce learnable compression to align features; (3) **Deep Fusion Mechanisms**, which integrate modalities within the model backbone for dynamic interaction; and (4) **Token-based Methods**, which unify the input space by treating vision as a native language. This section follows this evolutionary path, examining each stage's innovations and limitations.

This architectural progression is not merely a design choice but is deeply intertwined with the scaling laws and data efficiency of MLLMs. As highlighted in recent surveys <2306.13549>, the primary goal of these interfaces is to align the representation spaces of vision and language without disrupting the rich semantic knowledge embedded within the frozen LLM. For instance, linear projectors often struggle with high-resolution inputs due to the fixed context window of the LLM, whereas token-based methods like <EMU> or deep fusion like <FIBER> are designed to handle variable-length or interleaved inputs more naturally.

This evolution reflects a fundamental tension between efficiency and expressive power. For instance, while linear projectors offer minimal latency, they often struggle with the semantic misalignment inherent in the modality gap. In contrast, more complex connectors like Q-Former introduce significant parameter overhead but enable better feature compression. This trade-off drives research toward hybrid approaches that seek the best of both worlds.

#### 2.1 Linear Projectors

The foundational approach to bridging the modality gap relies on simple linear projection layers. <LLaVA> first demonstrated the viability of this method by connecting a visual encoder to an LLM using a single linear layer, establishing a baseline for parameter efficiency. This approach aligns visual features into the language embedding space with minimal overhead <LLaVA>, though it often struggles with the significant distributional discrepancy between modalities. <LLaVA> further validated this paradigm, showing that such simple projectors, when combined with high-quality instruction tuning data, can yield impressive multimodal capabilities. While highly efficient, these linear methods typically lack the capacity to compress or restructure visual information deeply, often leading to suboptimal performance on complex tasks requiring fine-grained visual understanding.

#### 2.2 Connector-Based Architectures

To address the limitations of simple projectors, a prominent approach involves the use of specialized modules to map visual tokens into the semantic space of the LLM. This section examines two dominant paradigms: Q-Former based approaches and Perceiver-based approaches.

##### 2.2.1 Q-Former based Approaches

<MiniGPT-4> utilizes Q-Former (inspired by BLIP-2) to learn a set of learnable queries (typically 32) that aggregate visual features from a frozen ViT before they are fed into the LLM. This method proved that treating visual input as a sequence of compressed, highly relevant features allows an otherwise frozen LLM to understand complex modalities, unlocking emergent capabilities like website generation from sketches. The mechanism of Q-Former in <MiniGPT-4> is particularly noteworthy for its role as a feature bottleneck. By freezing the visual encoder and training only the query vectors and the projection layer, the model learns to extract the most relevant visual information conditioned on the learnable queries. Internally, Q-Former employs a dual cross-attention mechanism: one set of cross-attention layers interacts the learnable queries with the image features to perform visual compression, while another set interacts the queries with text embeddings to facilitate multimodal fusion. This process effectively reduces the sequence length of visual tokens (e.g., from thousands of patch features to 32 queries), which significantly lowers the computational burden on the subsequent LLM. However, this aggressive compression can lead to information loss, especially for tasks requiring fine-grained spatial understanding.

The foundational training of Q-Former is heavily influenced by the BLIP-2 framework, which utilizes a mixture of three key objectives: Image-Text Contrastive (ITC) loss to align image and text representations in a shared space, ITM loss to fine-grained alignment via binary classification, and Masked Language Modeling (MLM) loss to predict masked text tokens conditioned on visual features. This multi-objective approach trains the queries to distill the most semantically relevant information for language generation. <BLIVA> attempts to mitigate the information loss of pure Q-Former approaches by retaining raw patch embeddings alongside the compressed queries, thereby preserving high-resolution visual details for text-rich images.

##### 2.2.2 Perceiver-based Approaches

Similarly, <KOSMOS-1> adopts a Perceiver-like architecture to handle interleaved multimodal inputs within a single Transformer. This architecture utilizes a cross-attention mechanism to iteratively compress input sequences into a fixed set of latent tokens. This design allows it to process variable-length multimodal data efficiently. By employing a mapping network and special boundary tokens, it effectively unifies modalities. However, reliance on complex, pre-trained feature compressors introduces significant parameter overhead and latency. In contrast to the single-pass aggregation of Q-Former, the Perceiver in <KOSMOS-1> uses a recurrent cross-attention mechanism that iteratively refines a set of latent tokens against the full visual input. The Perceiver updates its latent representation through multiple layers of cross-attention, allowing it to integrate information from the entire visual context progressively. This allows it to maintain a richer representation of the visual scene, albeit at the cost of increased inference time due to the iterative process. Perceiver-based methods in architectures like <KOSMOS-1> typically optimize a reconstruction loss (e.g., Mean Squared Error) on the visual features, prioritizing the retention of low-level visual fidelity over semantic alignment with language, which fundamentally shapes their respective compression strategies.

Comparative analysis reveals distinct performance trade-offs. While Q-Former-based connectors like <MiniGPT-4> offer lower latency and parameter counts, they may struggle with complex visual reasoning that requires detailed spatial awareness. This is largely due to their aggressive compression strategy, which reduces the sequence length of visual tokens (e.g., from thousands of patch features to 32 queries), significantly lowering the computational burden on the subsequent LLM but risking information loss. The Perceiver-based approach in <KOSMOS-1> provides better handling of variable-length inputs and potentially richer feature integration, but introduces higher computational costs due to iterative cross-attention layers. This hybrid approach highlights an ongoing design tension:
- **Compression vs. Fidelity**: Balancing the need for efficient, compressed representations that fit within LLM context windows against the necessity of preserving sufficient visual fidelity for complex reasoning tasks.
- **Latency vs. Integration**: Balancing the lower latency of single-pass aggregation against the richer feature integration (and higher cost) of iterative methods.

This trade-off between compression efficiency and computational cost defines a key tension in early connector designs.

#### 2.3 Deep Fusion Mechanisms

While the connector-based architectures discussed above rely on static compression to bridge the modality gap, recent research has shifted towards a paradigm of deep fusion. In reaction to the limitations of static pre-fusion, where visual context is fixed before generation begins, this approach seeks to integrate vision and language more deeply within the model backbone. <mPLUG-Owl2> introduces a "modality-adaptive module" that preserves modality-specific features, arguing that strict fusion can degrade text-only capabilities. It decouples the visual pathway while still sharing a universal language decoder. Conversely, <FIBER> argues for "Fusion in the Backbone." Instead of a separate connector, it interleaves cross-attention layers directly into the LLM’s encoder and decoder context. This deep fusion allows the model to reason about visual dependencies dynamically throughout the generation process, rather than relying on a static pre-fusion step. This trade-off—between specialized, compressed encoders (<MiniGPT-4>) and deep, integrated fusion (<FIBER>)—defines the current tension in interface design. Specifically, <mPLUG-Owl2> utilizes a "Modality-Adaptive Gating" mechanism within its cross-attention layers, allowing the model to selectively attend to visual or textual inputs depending on the current token, thereby maintaining modality integrity. In contrast, <FIBER> replaces standard self-attention layers in the LLM backbone with cross-attention layers that query visual tokens at every layer. This allows visual information to influence the generation of every token, not just the initial context, enabling a more dynamic and fine-grained interaction where visual reasoning is interleaved with linguistic generation.

#### 2.4 Token-Based Methods

While deep fusion focuses on integrating modalities within the model's processing layers, another distinct paradigm seeks to unify the input space itself by treating visual data as a sequence of tokens. This trend can be broadly categorized into two approaches: discrete tokenization and unified input spaces. These methods treat visual information as a sequence of tokens directly compatible with the LLM's processing pipeline, moving beyond simple projection to a unified token space.

##### Discrete Tokenization

This approach focuses on representing images as discrete codes for generative modeling. It involves mapping visual patches to a discrete codebook or generating visual tokens via a specialized tokenizer, allowing the model to process visual inputs much like text. <EMU> exemplifies this by utilizing a visual tokenizer to convert images into discrete tokens, which are then interleaved with text tokens in a unified sequence for generative modeling. These methods effectively treat vision as a "foreign language" that the LLM can natively understand.

##### Unified Input Spaces

This approach focuses on translating visual tasks into a language-like format for task execution. A notable evolution of this concept is found in <VisionLLM>, which proposes treating visual data as a "foreign language" via a generative decoder, allowing the LLM to process visual tokens using its native attention mechanisms. Unlike <EMU>'s focus on interleaved generation of text and image sequences, <VisionLLM> focuses on a unified generative framework for handling diverse visual tasks (e.g., detection, segmentation) by translating them into a language-like format. This strategy simplifies the architecture by removing the need for complex, modality-specific connectors, aligning with the trend toward architectural unification.

However, the efficacy of these architectural designs is intrinsically linked to the training data they require. This illustrates a broader trend: as architectures evolve from simple linear layers to complex, deep fusion mechanisms, they often demand more specialized and extensive training data to unlock their full potential. For instance, simple linear projectors like those in <LLaVA> can often achieve strong performance with pure instruction-tuning data, whereas connector-based architectures like <MiniGPT-4> typically benefit from a two-stage training pipeline involving large-scale pre-training on image-text pairs to learn the mapping between modalities before instruction tuning. Deep fusion models like <FIBER> require interleaved image-text data during pre-training to effectively learn the cross-attention mechanisms, whereas simpler projectors can rely solely on instruction-tuning data.

The trajectory of research suggests that while early interfaces focused on "how to connect" (via linear layers or projectors), the current focus is on "how to integrate." <mPLUG-Owl2> demonstrates that preserving modality-specific features is crucial for general intelligence, while <FIBER> suggests that dynamic fusion is key for fine-grained tasks. As we look at the evolution from <Visual Instruction Tuning>'s linear layer to <VisionLLM>'s generative decoder, the interface is transforming from a static bridge into a dynamic, intelligent router that determines which modality to prioritize and how to fuse context effectively.

The limitations of current interfaces are also becoming apparent in specific domains. For example, <BLIVA> addresses the failure of generalist connectors in text-rich images by explicitly injecting patch embeddings alongside learned query embeddings. This suggests that "one size fits all" interfaces may not be optimal. As the line between the visual encoder and the language model blurs, exemplified by <Downscaling Intelligence>'s proposal for visual extraction tuning, the modality interface is effectively becoming part of the reasoning engine itself, rather than a mere data conduit.

Furthermore, the interface is evolving to support explicit visual grounding, as seen in <KOSMOS-2>, which alters the output interface to align text with visual regions. This shift highlights that the modality interface is not just about feature alignment but also about enabling fine-grained, spatially-aware interaction. This represents a move beyond internal processing to the output interface, where generated text is spatially anchored to the visual input.

In conclusion, the modality interface has evolved from a trivial linear layer to a core architectural component defining the model's reasoning capabilities. This evolution directly addresses the 'modality gap' introduced in the first paragraph. Architectures like <VisionLLM> and token-based methods attempt to solve the distribution discrepancy by unifying visual and linguistic inputs into a shared generative space, effectively eliminating the gap by treating vision as a 'foreign language.' Conversely, deep fusion mechanisms like <FIBER> tackle the gap by dynamically integrating visual context throughout the reasoning process, rather than relying on a static pre-compression step. This trade-off is critical: heavy connectors like Q-Former often incur significant latency and parameter overhead, potentially limiting real-time application, whereas deep fusion strategies may offer superior reasoning capabilities but at a higher computational cost per token.

Beyond these architectural trade-offs, the field is increasingly recognizing that a single approach may be insufficient for all scenarios. As highlighted by the 'one size fits all' limitations in <BLIVA>, domain-specific challenges—such as processing text-rich images—require specialized adaptations, like retaining raw patch embeddings alongside compressed queries. Furthermore, the efficacy of these interfaces is heavily dependent on the training paradigm. For instance, connector-based models like <MiniGPT-4> often require extensive pre-training on image-text pairs to learn the mapping effectively, whereas token-based methods like <EMU> rely on the joint training of a visual tokenizer and the LLM. Consequently, a central open research question remains: how to design an interface that optimally balances inference efficiency with the rich, dynamic reasoning required for complex tasks, or whether the future lies in the unified generative spaces proposed by <VisionLLM> and <EMU>.

#### 2.5 Summary and Future Directions

In summary, the evolution of modality interfaces reflects a shift from static, specialized compression (e.g., Q-Former) toward dynamic, integrated fusion (e.g., <FIBER>) and unified generative spaces (e.g., <VisionLLM>). To clarify the trade-offs, we provide a comparative overview of the primary architectural paradigms discussed.

| Architecture | Parameter Efficiency | Inference Speed | Handling of Modality Gap | Data Requirements |
| :--- | :--- | :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **Linear Projector** | High | Fast | Poor (struggles with distributional discrepancy) | Moderate (relies on high-quality instruction tuning) |
| **Connector-based (e.g., Q-Former)** | Moderate (adds parameters) | Moderate (compression reduces LLM load but adds overhead) | Good (learns to aggregate features) | High (often requires large-scale image-text pre-training) |
| **Deep Fusion (e.g., <FIBER>)** | Low (integrated into LLM) | Slow (cross-attention at every layer) | Excellent (dynamic integration) | Very High (requires interleaved image-text data) |
| **Token-based (e.g., <EMU>)** | Moderate (requires tokenizer) | Moderate | Excellent (unifies input space) | Very High (joint training of tokenizer and LLM) |

This comparison highlights a recurring tension: compressing visual information to fit within LLM context windows versus preserving sufficient fidelity for complex reasoning. Looking ahead, a critical research direction is investigating whether 'dynamic sparsity' in fusion layers could resolve this tension. Preliminary work, such as the adaptive gating in <mPLUG-Owl2>, suggests that selectively prioritizing modalities is a promising path. Future work may explore whether models can learn to selectively activate only the most relevant cross-modal connections for a given task, thereby achieving the expressiveness of deep fusion while maintaining the efficiency of simpler architectures.

Beyond dynamic sparsity, several other avenues warrant exploration. First, the trend toward **Unified Multimodal Architectures** suggests a future where the distinction between the visual encoder and the language model blurs, potentially evolving into a single, native multimodal model from the ground up, rather than bridging separate components. Second, as models grow, developing **Efficient Fine-tuning Strategies** becomes crucial to adapt these powerful interfaces to specialized domains (e.g., medical imaging, scientific analysis) without prohibitive computational costs. Finally, inspired by benchmarks like <BLINK-Twice> and <Hyperphantasia>, there is a growing need to move beyond passive perception toward **Active and Causal Reasoning**, where models can actively interrogate visual scenes and reason about physical or causal relationships, a capability essential for next-generation AI agents.

    ### 2. Evolution of Modality Interfaces

The architectural design of the modality interface—the bridge that connects visual encoders with Large Language Models (LLMs)—has evolved into a central battleground for defining the capabilities of Multimodal Large Language Models (MLLMs) <2306.13549>. Unlike <EMU>'s focus on interleaved generation of text and image sequences, <VisionLLM> focuses on a unified generative framework for handling diverse visual tasks (e.g., detection, segmentation) by translating them into a language-like format. This strategy simplifies the architecture by removing the need for complex, modality-specific connectors, aligning with the trend toward architectural unification.

However, the efficacy of these architectural designs is intrinsically linked to the training data they require.
