This trade-off between compression efficiency and computational cost defines a key tension in early connector designs. While effective, these static compression methods risk information loss, motivating a shift towards dynamic integration.

#### 2.3 Deep Fusion Mechanisms

While the connector-based architectures discussed above rely on static compression to bridge the modality gap, recent research has shifted towards a paradigm of deep fusion. This shift is motivated by the limitations of static pre-fusion, where aggressive compression strategies (e.g., reducing thousands of visual patches to a few dozen queries in Q-Former) risk significant information loss, as noted in surveys <A survey on multimodal large language models>. Consequently, deep fusion seeks to integrate vision and language more deeply within the model backbone, enabling dynamic interaction throughout the generation process. <mPLUG-Owl2> introduces a "modality-adaptive module" that preserves modality-specific features, arguing that strict fusion can degrade text-only capabilities. It decouples the visual pathway while still sharing a universal language decoder. Conversely, <FIBER> argues for "Fusion in the Backbone." Instead of a separate connector, it interleaves cross-attention layers directly into the LLM’s encoder and decoder context. This deep fusion allows the model to reason about visual dependencies dynamically throughout the generation process, rather than relying on a static pre-fusion step. This trade-off—between specialized, compressed encoders (<MiniGPT-4>) and deep, integrated fusion (<FIBER>)—defines the current tension in interface design. Specifically, <mPLUG-Owl2> utilizes a "Modality-Adaptive Gating" mechanism within its cross-attention layers, allowing the model to selectively attend to visual or textual inputs depending on the current token, thereby maintaining modality integrity. In contrast, <FIBER> replaces standard self-attention layers in the LLM backbone with cross-attention layers that query visual tokens at every layer. Architecturally, this means the Key and Value (K, V) matrices are derived exclusively from visual tokens, while the Query (Q) matrix comes from the text token representations. This forces the model to ground every text token generation on visual evidence, rather than allowing text tokens to attend only to previous text tokens (autoregressive self-attention). This shift has profound implications for training dynamics: by bypassing the standard self-attention mechanism, <FIBER> alters the gradient flow, potentially requiring careful tuning to maintain stability and prevent the degradation of the LLM's linguistic reasoning capabilities. This enables "early fusion," where visual context is available at every token generation step, allowing visual information to influence the generation of every token, not just the initial context, enabling a more dynamic and fine-grained interaction where visual reasoning is interleaved with linguistic generation.

#### 2.4 Token-Based Methods

In contrast to the deep fusion approach, which integrates modalities within the model's processing layers, another distinct paradigm seeks to unify the input space itself by treating visual data as a sequence of tokens. This trend can be broadly categorized into two approaches: discrete tokenization and unified input spaces. These methods treat visual information as a sequence of tokens directly compatible with the LLM's processing pipeline, moving beyond simple projection to a unified token space.

##### 2.4.1 Discrete Tokenization

This approach focuses on representing images as discrete codes for generative modeling. It involves mapping visual patches to a discrete codebook or generating visual tokens via a specialized tokenizer, allowing the model to process visual inputs much like text. <EMU> exemplifies this by utilizing a visual tokenizer to convert images into discrete tokens, which are then interleaved with text tokens in a unified sequence for generative modeling. These methods effectively treat vision as a "foreign language" that the LLM can natively understand.

##### 2.4.2 Unified Input Spaces

This approach focuses on translating visual tasks into a language-like format for task execution. A notable evolution of this concept is found in <VisionLLM>, which proposes treating visual data as a "foreign language" via a generative decoder. This is achieved by translating diverse visual tasks into a language-like format: for instance, object detection is reformulated as a prompt such as "Detect <object>". The LLM then generates a structured sequence of special tokens that are decoded into structured outputs like bounding boxes or segmentation masks. This strategy differs from <EMU>'s focus on interleaved generation of text and image sequences; instead, <VisionLLM> focuses on a unified generative framework for handling diverse visual tasks. This strategy simplifies the architecture by removing the need for complex, modality-specific connectors, aligning with the trend toward architectural unification.

#### 2.5 Architectural Impact on Data Efficiency

The efficacy of these architectural designs is intrinsically linked to the training data they require. This illustrates a broader trend: as architectures evolve from simple linear layers to complex, deep fusion mechanisms, they often demand more specialized and extensive training data to unlock their full potential. For instance, simple linear projectors like those in <LLaVA> can often achieve strong performance with pure instruction-tuning data, whereas connector-based architectures like <MiniGPT-4> typically benefit from a two-stage training pipeline involving large-scale pre-training on image-text pairs to learn the mapping between modalities before instruction tuning <2306.13549>. Deep fusion models like <FIBER> require interleaved image-text data during pre-training to effectively learn the cross-attention mechanisms, whereas simpler projectors can rely solely on instruction-tuning data. This dependency highlights that architectural complexity must be matched by data complexity to realize advanced capabilities.

#### 2.6 Output Interfaces and Grounding

While previous sections focused on the input interface, the evolution of modality interfaces also encompasses the output side, enabling models to generate structured and grounded visual responses. A prominent direction is explicit visual grounding, where generated text is spatially anchored to the visual input. <KOSMOS-2> exemplifies this by altering the output interface to align text with specific visual regions, moving beyond internal processing to produce spatially-aware outputs. Similarly, <VisionLLM> proposes treating visual data as a "foreign language" to generate structured outputs for diverse tasks like detection and segmentation, effectively unifying the generative interface across modalities.

#### 2.7 Domain-Specific Limitations and Robustness

This evolution is further driven by the need to address domain-specific limitations. For example, <BLIVA> addresses the failure of generalist connectors in text-rich images by explicitly injecting patch embeddings alongside learned query embeddings, suggesting that "one size fits all" interfaces may not be optimal. As the line between the visual encoder and the language model blurs, exemplified by <Downscaling Intelligence>'s proposal for visual extraction tuning, the modality interface is effectively becoming part of the reasoning engine itself, rather than a mere data conduit. This shift is critical, as recent benchmarks like <BLINK: Multimodal Large Language Models Can See but Not Perceive> and <BLINK-Twice: You see, but do you observe?> reveal that despite architectural advances, MLLMs still struggle with core visual perception and observation, often relying on linguistic shortcuts rather than true visual reasoning. This underscores that the ultimate goal of the modality interface is not just alignment, but enabling genuine visual understanding.

#### 2.7 Summary and Future Outlook

The evolution of modality interfaces traces a clear trajectory from static, simple linear projections to dynamic, deeply integrated fusion mechanisms. This progression is fundamentally driven by the need to bridge the modality gap while balancing the critical trade-offs between computational efficiency and expressive power. Early linear projectors offered minimal overhead but struggled with semantic misalignment, while connector-based architectures like Q-Former introduced learnable compression at the cost of potential information loss and parameter overhead. The subsequent shift towards deep fusion mechanisms, such as those in <FIBER>, enables more dynamic and interleaved reasoning but at a higher computational cost. Finally, token-based methods aim for a unified input space, treating vision as a native language for the LLM.

Looking ahead, the trajectory of modality interfaces points toward greater unification and dynamic interaction. The tension between efficiency and expressiveness remains a central challenge, suggesting that future architectures will likely be hybrid, combining the strengths of different stages—for instance, leveraging efficient compression for initial context while enabling deep fusion for complex reasoning steps. Furthermore, as the interface becomes more integrated into the reasoning engine itself, the line between perception and reasoning will continue to blur. This evolution necessitates a deeper understanding of how these architectural choices impact not just performance on benchmarks, but also the model's fundamental capabilities in spatial reasoning, visual grounding, and robustness against adversarial attacks. The next section will explore how these advanced interface designs enable complex multimodal reasoning capabilities.

    ### 2. Evolution of Modality Interfaces

The architectural design of the modality interface—the bridge that connects visual encoders with Large Language Models (LLMs)—has evolved into a central battleground for defining the capabilities of Multimodal Large Language Models (MLLMs) <2306.13549>. This evolution traces a clear trajectory from simple, static bridges to complex, dynamic integration mechanisms. Early efforts, such as <LLaVA>, established the viability of simple linear projection layers, but their inability to bridge the "modality gap"—the significant discrepancy in feature distributions and semantic granularity between visual and linguistic modalities <2306.13549>—drove the development of more sophisticated architectures. This progression can be viewed as a series of evolutionary stages: (1) **Linear Projectors**, the foundational baseline; (2) **Connector-based Architectures** (e.g., Q-Former), which introduce learnable compression to align features; (3) **Deep Fusion Mechanisms**, which integrate modalities within the model backbone for dynamic interaction; and (4) **Token-based Methods**, which unify the input space by treating vision as a native language. This section follows this evolutionary path, examining each stage's innovations and limitations.

This architectural progression is not merely a design choice but is deeply intertwined with the scaling laws and data efficiency of MLLMs. As highlighted in recent surveys <A survey on multimodal large language models>, the primary goal of these interfaces is to align the representation spaces of vision and language without disrupting the rich semantic knowledge embedded within the frozen LLM. For instance, linear projectors often struggle with high-resolution inputs due to the fixed context window of the LLM, whereas token-based methods like <EMU> or deep fusion like <FIBER> are designed to handle variable-length or interleaved inputs more naturally.

This evolution reflects a fundamental tension between efficiency and expressive power. For instance, while linear projectors offer minimal latency, they often struggle with the semantic misalignment inherent in the modality gap. In contrast, more complex connectors like Q-Former introduce significant parameter overhead but enable better feature compression <BLIP-2>. This trade-off drives research toward hybrid approaches that seek the best of both worlds, such as the decoupled visual pathway in <mPLUG-Owl2>. These architectural choices are not merely technical details; they fundamentally shape the model's data efficiency and scaling laws, determining how well the interface aligns representation spaces without disrupting the rich semantic knowledge embedded within the frozen LLM.

#### 2.1 Linear Projectors

The foundational approach to bridging the modality gap relies on simple linear projection layers. <LLaVA> first demonstrated the viability of this method by connecting a visual encoder to an LLM using a single linear layer, establishing a baseline for parameter efficiency. This approach aligns visual features into the language embedding space with minimal overhead, though it often struggles with the significant distributional discrepancy between modalities. However, when combined with high-quality instruction tuning data, such simple projectors can yield impressive multimodal capabilities. While highly efficient, these linear methods typically lack the capacity to compress or restructure visual information deeply, often leading to suboptimal performance on complex tasks requiring fine-grained visual understanding.

#### 2.2 Connector-Based Architectures

To address the limitations of simple projectors, a prominent approach involves the use of specialized modules to map visual tokens into the semantic space of the LLM. This section examines two dominant paradigms: Q-Former based approaches and Perceiver-based approaches. These architectures effectively act as 'bottlenecks' or 'compressors', attempting to distill the most relevant visual information into a fixed number of tokens that the LLM can process efficiently.

##### 2.2.1 Q-Former based Approaches

<MiniGPT-4> utilizes Q-Former (inspired by <BLIP-2>) to learn a set of learnable queries (typically 32) that aggregate visual features from a frozen ViT before they are fed into the LLM. This method proved that treating visual input as a sequence of compressed, highly relevant features allows an otherwise frozen LLM to understand complex modalities, unlocking emergent capabilities like website generation from sketches. The mechanism of Q-Former in <MiniGPT-4> is particularly noteworthy for its role as a feature bottleneck. By freezing the visual encoder and training only the query vectors and the projection layer, the model learns to extract the most relevant visual information conditioned on the learnable queries. Internally, Q-Former employs a dual cross-attention mechanism: one set of cross-attention layers interacts the learnable queries with the image features to perform visual compression, while another set interacts the queries with text embeddings to facilitate multimodal fusion. This process effectively reduces the sequence length of visual tokens (e.g., from thousands of patch features to 32 queries), which significantly lowers the computational burden on the subsequent LLM. However, this aggressive compression can lead to information loss, especially for tasks requiring fine-grained spatial understanding. This contrasts with methods like Perceiver-based architectures, which initialize latent tokens directly from the input sequence and iteratively refine them, rather than relying on static, learnable queries.

The foundational training of Q-Former is heavily influenced by the BLIP-2 framework, which utilizes a mixture of three key objectives: Image-Text Contrastive (ITC) loss to align image and text representations in a shared space, ITM loss to fine-grained alignment via binary classification, and Masked Language Modeling (MLM) loss to predict masked text tokens conditioned on visual features. Specifically, the ITC objective aligns the representation of the [CLS] token from the text encoder with the pooled output of the visual queries, ensuring a global correspondence between the image and the caption. The ITM objective acts as a fine-grained filter, using a binary classifier to determine if a pair of image and text is matched, which helps the model distinguish between positive and negative pairs with high precision. Finally, the MLM objective enables the model to learn cross-modal relationships by masking text tokens and requiring the model to predict them using the information provided by the visual queries. This objective is particularly crucial for the 'feature bottleneck' concept: by forcing the 32 queries to reconstruct masked text, it compels them to retain the semantic information necessary for language generation, rather than just raw visual features. This multi-objective approach trains the queries to distill the most semantically relevant information for language generation. <BLIVA> attempts to mitigate the information loss of pure Q-Former approaches by retaining raw patch embeddings alongside the compressed queries, thereby preserving high-resolution visual details for text-rich images.

##### 2.2.2 Perceiver-based Approaches

Similarly, <KOSMOS-1> adopts a Perceiver-like architecture to handle interleaved multimodal inputs within a single Transformer. This architecture utilizes a cross-attention mechanism to iteratively compress input sequences into a fixed set of latent tokens. This design allows it to process variable-length multimodal data efficiently. By employing a mapping network and special boundary tokens, it effectively unifies modalities. However, reliance on complex, pre-trained feature compressors introduces significant parameter overhead and latency. In contrast to the single-pass aggregation of Q-Former, the Perceiver in <KOSMOS-1> uses a recurrent cross-attention mechanism that iteratively refines a set of latent tokens against the full visual input. The Perceiver updates its latent representation through multiple layers of cross-attention, allowing it to integrate information from the entire visual context progressively. This allows it to maintain a richer representation of the visual scene, albeit at the cost of increased inference time due to the iterative process. Perceiver-based methods in architectures like <KOSMOS-1> typically optimize a reconstruction loss (e.g., Mean Squared Error) on the visual features, prioritizing the retention of low-level visual fidelity over semantic alignment with language, which fundamentally shapes their respective compression strategies.

Comparative analysis reveals distinct performance trade-offs. While Q-Former-based connectors like <MiniGPT-4> offer lower latency and parameter counts, they may struggle with complex visual reasoning that requires detailed spatial awareness. This is largely due to their aggressive compression strategy, which reduces the sequence length of visual tokens (e.g., from thousands of patch features to 32 queries), significantly lowering the computational burden on the subsequent LLM but risking information loss. The Perceiver-based approach in <KOSMOS-1> provides better handling of variable-length inputs and potentially richer feature integration, but introduces higher computational costs due to iterative cross-attention layers. This hybrid approach highlights an ongoing design tension:
- **Compression vs. Fidelity**: Balancing the need for efficient, compressed representations that fit within LLM context windows against the necessity of preserving sufficient visual fidelity for complex reasoning tasks.
- **Latency vs. Integration**: Balancing the lower latency of single-pass aggregation against the richer feature integration (and higher cost) of iterative methods.

This trade-off between compression efficiency and computational cost defines a key tension in early connector designs. While effective, these static compression methods risk information loss, motivating a shift towards dynamic integration.

#### 2.3 Deep Fusion Mechanisms

While the connector-based architectures discussed above rely on static compression to bridge the modality gap, recent research has shifted towards a paradigm of deep fusion. This shift is motivated by the limitations of static pre-fusion, where aggressive compression strategies (e.g., reducing thousands of visual patches to a few dozen queries in Q-Former) risk significant information loss, as noted in surveys <A survey on multimodal large language models>. Consequently, deep fusion seeks to integrate vision and language more deeply within the model backbone, enabling dynamic interaction throughout the generation process. <mPLUG-Owl2> introduces a "modality-adaptive module" that preserves modality-specific features, arguing that strict fusion can degrade text-only capabilities. It decouples the visual pathway while still sharing a universal language decoder. Conversely, <FIBER> argues for "Fusion in the Backbone." Instead of a separate connector, it interleaves cross-attention layers directly into the LLM’s encoder and decoder context. This deep fusion allows the model to reason about visual dependencies dynamically throughout the generation process, rather than relying on a static pre-fusion step. This trade-off—between specialized, compressed encoders (<MiniGPT-4>) and deep, integrated fusion (<FIBER>)—defines the current tension in interface design. Specifically, <mPLUG-Owl2> utilizes a "Modality-Adaptive Gating" mechanism within its cross-attention layers, allowing the model to selectively attend to visual or textual inputs depending on the current token, thereby maintaining modality integrity. In contrast, <FIBER> replaces standard self-attention layers in the LLM backbone with cross-attention layers that query visual tokens at every layer. Architecturally, this means the Key and Value (K, V) matrices are derived exclusively from visual tokens, while the Query (Q) matrix comes from the text token representations. This forces the model to ground every text token generation on visual evidence, rather than allowing text tokens to attend only to previous text tokens (autoregressive self-attention). This shift has profound implications for training dynamics: by bypassing the standard self-attention mechanism, <FIBER> alters the gradient flow, potentially requiring careful tuning to maintain stability and prevent the degradation of the LLM's linguistic reasoning capabilities. This architectural change may necessitate specialized initialization strategies or learning rate schedules to ensure stable convergence, a challenge less pronounced in connector-based approaches. This enables "early fusion," where visual context is available at every token generation step, allowing visual information to influence the generation of every token, not just the initial context, enabling a more dynamic and fine-grained interaction where visual reasoning is interleaved with linguistic generation.

#### 2.4 Token-Based Methods

In contrast to the deep fusion approach, which integrates modalities via dynamic cross-attention within the model's processing layers, another distinct paradigm seeks to unify the input space itself by treating visual data as a sequence of tokens. This trend can be broadly categorized into two approaches: discrete tokenization and unified input spaces. These methods treat visual information as a sequence of tokens directly compatible with the LLM's processing pipeline, moving beyond simple projection to a unified token space. Conceptually, this represents a shift from the dynamic, interleaved reasoning of deep fusion (e.g., <FIBER>) to a static unification of the input representation, where vision is treated as a 'foreign language' natively understood by the LLM (e.g., <VisionLLM>).

##### 2.4.1 Discrete Tokenization

This approach focuses on representing images as discrete codes for generative modeling. It involves mapping visual patches to a discrete codebook or generating visual tokens via a specialized tokenizer, allowing the model to process visual inputs much like text. <EMU> exemplifies this by utilizing a visual tokenizer to convert images into discrete tokens, which are then interleaved with text tokens in a unified sequence for generative modeling. These methods effectively treat vision as a "foreign language" that the LLM can natively understand.

##### 2.4.2 Unified Input Spaces

This approach focuses on translating visual tasks into a language-like format for task execution. A notable evolution of this concept is found in <VisionLLM>, which proposes treating visual data as a "foreign language" via a generative decoder. This is achieved by translating diverse visual tasks into a language-like format: for instance, object detection is reformulated as a prompt such as "Detect <object>". The LLM then generates a structured sequence of special tokens that are decoded into structured outputs like bounding boxes or segmentation masks. This strategy differs from <EMU>'s focus on interleaved generation of text and image sequences; instead, <VisionLLM> focuses on a unified generative framework for handling diverse visual tasks. This strategy simplifies the architecture by removing the need for complex, modality-specific connectors, aligning with the trend toward architectural unification.

#### 2.5 Architectural Impact on Data Efficiency

The efficacy of these architectural designs is intrinsically linked to the training data they require. This illustrates a broader trend: as architectures evolve from simple linear layers to complex, deep fusion mechanisms, they often demand more specialized and extensive training data to unlock their full potential. For instance, simple linear projectors like those in <LLaVA> can often achieve strong performance with pure instruction-tuning data, whereas connector-based architectures like <MiniGPT-4> typically benefit from a two-stage training pipeline involving large-scale pre-training on image-text pairs to learn the mapping between modalities before instruction tuning <2306.13549>. Deep fusion models like <FIBER> require interleaved image-text data during pre-training to effectively learn the cross-attention mechanisms, whereas simpler projectors can rely solely on instruction-tuning data. This dependency highlights that architectural complexity must be matched by data complexity to realize advanced capabilities. Specifically, while connector-based models often leverage massive web-scale datasets like LAION-5B for pre-training the compression module, deep fusion models necessitate curated interleaved corpora (e.g., from WebLI or internal datasets) to train the integrated cross-attention layers effectively. This shift implies that as models move towards deeper integration, the data requirement shifts from simple image-text pairs to more complex, sequential multimodal data, significantly increasing the cost and complexity of pre-training.

#### 2.6 Output Interfaces and Grounding

Parallel to the evolution of input interfaces, the output interfaces have also evolved to enable more structured and grounded responses, moving beyond pure text generation. A prominent direction is explicit visual grounding, where generated text is spatially anchored to the visual input. <KOSMOS-2> exemplifies this by altering the output interface to align text with specific visual regions, moving beyond internal processing to produce spatially-aware outputs. Similarly, <VisionLLM> proposes treating visual data as a "foreign language" to generate structured outputs for diverse tasks like detection and segmentation, effectively unifying the generative interface across modalities.

#### 2.7 Specialized Adaptations and Robustness Challenges

This evolution is further driven by the need to address domain-specific limitations. For example, <BLIVA> addresses the failure of generalist connectors in text-rich images by explicitly injecting patch embeddings alongside learned query embeddings, suggesting that "one size fits all" interfaces may not be optimal. As the line between the visual encoder and the language model blurs, exemplified by <Downscaling Intelligence>'s proposal for visual extraction tuning, the modality interface is effectively becoming part of the reasoning engine itself, rather than a mere data conduit. This shift is critical, as recent benchmarks like <BLINK: Multimodal Large Language Models Can See but Not Perceive> and <BLINK-Twice: You see, but do you observe?> reveal that despite architectural advances, MLLMs still struggle with core visual perception and observation, often relying on linguistic shortcuts rather than true visual reasoning. This underscores that the ultimate goal of the modality interface is not just alignment, but enabling genuine visual understanding.

#### 2.7 Summary and Future Outlook

The evolution of modality interfaces traces a clear trajectory from static, simple linear projections to dynamic, deeply integrated fusion mechanisms. This progression is fundamentally driven by the need to bridge the modality gap while balancing the critical trade-offs between computational efficiency and expressive power. Early linear projectors offered minimal overhead but struggled with semantic misalignment, while connector-based architectures like Q-Former introduced learnable compression at the cost of potential information loss and parameter overhead. The subsequent shift towards deep fusion mechanisms, such as those in <FIBER>, enables more dynamic and interleaved reasoning but at a higher computational cost. Finally, token-based methods aim for a unified input space, treating vision as a native language for the LLM.

Looking ahead, the trajectory of modality interfaces points toward greater unification and dynamic interaction. The tension between efficiency and expressiveness remains a central challenge, suggesting that future architectures will likely be hybrid, combining the strengths of different stages—for instance, leveraging efficient compression for initial context while enabling deep fusion for complex reasoning steps. Furthermore, as the interface becomes more integrated into the reasoning engine itself, the line between perception and reasoning will continue to blur. This evolution necessitates a deeper understanding of how these architectural choices impact not just performance on benchmarks, but also the model's fundamental capabilities in spatial reasoning, visual grounding, and robustness against adversarial attacks. The next section will explore how these advanced interface designs enable complex multimodal reasoning capabilities.

    ### 2. Evolution of Modality Interfaces

The architectural design of the modality interface—the bridge that connects visual encoders with Large Language Models (LLMs)—has evolved into a central battleground for defining the capabilities of Multimodal Large Language Models (MLLMs) <2306.13549>. However, reliance on complex, pre-trained feature compressors introduces significant parameter overhead and latency. In contrast to the single-pass aggregation of Q-Former, the Perceiver in <KOSMOS-1> uses a recurrent cross-attention mechanism that iteratively refines a set of latent tokens against the full visual input. Specifically, the Perceiver architecture consists of a repeating block of cross-attention layers followed by self-attention layers. In the cross-attention layer, the latent tokens (queries) attend to the visual features (keys/values), allowing the model to distill information from the high-resolution visual input into the compressed latent space. Subsequently, self-attention layers among the latent tokens allow them to communicate and integrate the gathered information. This process is repeated multiple times, progressively refining the latent representation. This iterative approach allows it to maintain a richer representation of the visual scene, albeit at the cost of increased inference time. This architectural difference in training objectives is also notable: while Q-Former relies on the BLIP-2 objectives (ITC, ITM, MLM) to align compressed features with language semantics, Perceiver-based methods in architectures like <KOSMOS-1> typically optimize a reconstruction loss (e.g., Mean Squared Error) on the visual features, prioritizing the retention of low-level visual fidelity over semantic alignment with language, which fundamentally shapes their respective compression strategies.

Comparative analysis reveals distinct performance trade-offs. While Q-Former-based connectors like <MiniGPT-4> offer lower latency and parameter counts, they may struggle with complex visual reasoning that requires detailed spatial awareness. This is largely due to their aggressive compression strategy, which reduces the sequence length of visual tokens (e.g., from thousands of patch features to 32 queries), significantly lowering the computational burden on the subsequent LLM but risking information loss. The Perceiver-based approach in <KOSMOS-1> provides better handling of variable-length inputs and potentially richer feature integration, but introduces higher computational costs due to iterative cross-attention layers. This hybrid approach highlights an ongoing design tension:
- **Compression vs. Fidelity**: Balancing the need for efficient, compressed representations that fit within LLM context windows against the necessity of preserving sufficient visual fidelity for complex reasoning tasks.
- **Latency vs. Integration**: Balancing the lower latency of single-pass aggregation against the richer feature integration (and higher cost) of iterative methods.

This trade-off between compression efficiency and computational cost defines a key tension in early connector designs. While effective, these static compression methods risk information loss, motivating a shift towards dynamic integration.

#### 2.3 Deep Fusion Mechanisms

While the connector-based architectures discussed above rely on static compression to bridge the modality gap, recent research has shifted towards a paradigm of deep fusion. This shift is motivated by the limitations of static pre-fusion, where aggressive compression strategies (e.g., reducing thousands of visual patches to a few dozen queries in Q-Former) risk significant information loss, as noted in surveys <A survey on multimodal large language models>. Consequently, deep fusion seeks to integrate vision and language more deeply within the model backbone, enabling dynamic interaction throughout the generation process. <mPLUG-Owl2> introduces a "modality-adaptive module" that preserves modality-specific features, arguing that strict fusion can degrade text-only capabilities. It decouples the visual pathway while still sharing a universal language decoder. Conversely, <FIBER> argues for "Fusion in the Backbone." Instead of a separate connector, it interleaves cross-attention layers directly into the LLM’s encoder and decoder context. This deep fusion allows the model to reason about visual dependencies dynamically throughout the generation process, rather than relying on a static pre-fusion step. This trade-off—between specialized, compressed encoders (<MiniGPT-4>) and deep, integrated fusion (<FIBER>)—defines the current tension in interface design. Specifically, <mPLUG-Owl2> utilizes a "Modality-Adaptive Gating" mechanism within its cross-attention layers, allowing the model to selectively attend to visual or textual inputs depending on the current token, thereby maintaining modality integrity. In contrast, <FIBER> replaces standard self-attention layers in the LLM backbone with cross-attention layers that query visual tokens at every layer. Architecturally, this means the Key and Value (K, V) matrices are derived exclusively from visual tokens, while the Query (Q) matrix comes from the text token representations. This forces the model to ground every text token generation on visual evidence, rather than allowing text tokens to attend only to previous text tokens (autoregressive self-attention). This shift has profound implications for training dynamics: by bypassing the standard self-attention mechanism, <FIBER> alters the gradient flow, potentially requiring careful tuning to maintain stability and prevent the degradation of the LLM's linguistic reasoning capabilities. This architectural change may necessitate specialized initialization strategies or learning rate schedules to ensure stable convergence, a challenge less pronounced in connector-based approaches <FIBER>. This enables "early fusion," where visual context is available at every token generation step, allowing visual information to influence the generation of every token, not just the initial context, enabling a more dynamic and fine-grained interaction where visual reasoning is interleaved with linguistic generation.

#### 2.4 Token-Based Methods

In contrast to the deep fusion approach, which integrates modalities via dynamic cross-attention within the model's processing layers, another distinct paradigm seeks to unify the input space itself by treating visual data as a sequence of tokens. This trend diverges into two primary applications: generative modeling of interleaved sequences and task translation for structured outputs. These methods treat visual information as a sequence of tokens directly compatible with the LLM's processing pipeline, moving beyond simple projection to a unified token space. Conceptually, this represents a shift from the dynamic, interleaved reasoning of deep fusion (e.g., <FIBER>) to a static unification of the input representation, where vision is treated as a 'foreign language' natively understood by the LLM (e.g., <VisionLLM>).

##### 2.4.1 Discrete Tokenization

This approach focuses on representing images as discrete codes for generative modeling. It involves mapping visual patches to a discrete codebook or generating visual tokens via a specialized tokenizer, allowing the model to process visual inputs much like text. <EMU> exemplifies this by utilizing a visual tokenizer to convert images into discrete tokens, which are then interleaved with text tokens in a unified sequence for generative modeling. These methods effectively treat vision as a "foreign language" that the LLM can natively understand.

##### 2.4.2 Unified Input Spaces

This approach focuses on translating visual tasks into a language-like format for task execution. A notable evolution of this concept is found in <VisionLLM>, which proposes treating visual data as a "foreign language" via a generative decoder. This is achieved by translating diverse visual tasks into a language-like format: for instance, object detection is reformulated as a prompt such as "Detect <object>". The LLM then generates a structured sequence of special tokens that are decoded into structured outputs like bounding boxes or segmentation masks. This strategy differs from <EMU>'s focus on interleaved generation of text and image sequences; instead, <VisionLLM> focuses on a unified generative framework for handling diverse visual tasks. This strategy simplifies the architecture by removing the need for complex, modality-specific connectors, aligning with the trend toward architectural unification.

#### 2.5 Architectural Impact on Data Efficiency

The efficacy of these architectural designs is intrinsically linked to the training data they require. This illustrates a broader trend: as architectures evolve from simple linear layers to complex, deep fusion mechanisms, they often demand more specialized and extensive training data to unlock their full potential. For instance, simple linear projectors like those in <LLaVA> can often achieve strong performance with pure instruction-tuning data, whereas connector-based architectures like <MiniGPT-4> typically benefit from a two-stage training pipeline involving large-scale pre-training on image-text pairs to learn the mapping between modalities before instruction tuning <2306.13549>. Deep fusion models like <FIBER> require interleaved image-text data during pre-training to effectively learn the cross-attention mechanisms, whereas simpler projectors can rely solely on instruction-tuning data. This dependency highlights that architectural complexity must be matched by data complexity to realize advanced capabilities. Specifically, while connector-based models often leverage massive web-scale datasets like LAION-5B for pre-training the compression module, deep fusion models necessitate curated interleaved corpora (e.g., from WebLI or internal datasets) to train the integrated cross-attention layers effectively. This shift implies that as models move towards deeper integration, the data requirement shifts from simple image-text pairs to more complex, sequential multimodal data, significantly increasing the cost and complexity of pre-training.

#### 2.6 Output Interfaces and Grounding

Parallel to the evolution of input interfaces, the output interfaces have also evolved to enable more structured and grounded responses, moving beyond pure text generation. A prominent direction is explicit visual grounding, where generated text is spatially anchored to the visual input. <KOSMOS-2> exemplifies this by altering the output interface to align text with specific visual regions, moving beyond internal processing to produce spatially-aware outputs. Similarly, <VisionLLM> proposes treating visual data as a "foreign language" to generate structured outputs for diverse tasks like detection and segmentation, effectively unifying the generative interface across modalities.

#### 2.7 Specialized Adaptations and Robustness Challenges

This evolution is further driven by the need to address domain-specific limitations. For example, <BLIVA> addresses the failure of generalist connectors in text-rich images by explicitly injecting patch embeddings alongside learned query embeddings, suggesting that "one size fits all" interfaces may not be optimal. As the line between the visual encoder and the language model blurs, exemplified by <Downscaling Intelligence>'s proposal for visual extraction tuning, the modality interface is effectively becoming part of the reasoning engine itself, rather than a mere data conduit. This shift is critical, as recent benchmarks like <BLINK: Multimodal Large Language Models Can See but Not Perceive> and <BLINK-Twice: You see, but do you observe?> reveal that despite architectural advances, MLLMs still struggle with core visual perception and observation, often relying on linguistic shortcuts rather than true visual reasoning. This underscores that the ultimate goal of the modality interface is not just alignment, but enabling genuine visual understanding.

#### 2.8 Summary and Future Outlook

The evolution of modality interfaces traces a clear trajectory from static, simple linear projections to dynamic, deeply integrated fusion mechanisms. This progression is fundamentally driven by the need to bridge the modality gap while balancing the critical trade-offs between computational efficiency and expressive power. Early linear projectors offered minimal overhead but struggled with semantic misalignment, while connector-based architectures like Q-Former introduced learnable compression at the cost of potential information loss and parameter overhead. The subsequent shift towards deep fusion mechanisms, such as those in <FIBER>, enables more dynamic and interleaved reasoning but at a higher computational cost. Finally, token-based methods aim for a unified input space, treating vision as a native language for the LLM.

Looking ahead, the trajectory of modality interfaces points toward greater unification and dynamic interaction. The tension between efficiency and expressiveness remains a central challenge, suggesting that future architectures will likely be hybrid, combining the strengths of different stages—for instance, leveraging efficient compression for initial context while enabling deep fusion for complex reasoning steps. Furthermore, as the interface becomes more integrated into the reasoning engine itself, the line between perception and reasoning will continue to blur. This evolution necessitates a deeper understanding of how these architectural choices impact not just performance on benchmarks, but also the model's fundamental capabilities in spatial reasoning, visual grounding, and robustness against adversarial attacks. The next section will explore how these advanced interface designs enable complex multimodal reasoning capabilities.

    ### 2. Evolution of Modality Interfaces

The architectural design of the modality interface—the bridge that connects visual encoders with Large Language Models (LLMs)—has evolved into a central battleground for defining the capabilities of Multimodal Large Language Models (MLLMs) <A survey on multimodal large language models>. This evolution traces a clear trajectory from simple, static bridges to complex, dynamic integration mechanisms. Early efforts, such as <LLaVA>, established the viability of simple linear projection layers, but their inability to bridge the "modality gap"—the significant discrepancy in feature distributions and semantic granularity between visual and linguistic modalities <2306.13549>—drove the development of more sophisticated architectures. This progression can be viewed as a series of evolutionary stages: (1) **Linear Projectors**, the foundational baseline; (2) **Connector-based Architectures** (e.g., Q-Former), which introduce learnable compression to align features; (3) **Deep Fusion Mechanisms**, which integrate modalities within the model backbone for dynamic interaction; and (4) **Token-based Methods**, which unify the input space by treating vision as a native language. This section follows this evolutionary path, examining each stage's innovations and limitations.

This architectural progression is not merely a design choice but is deeply intertwined with the scaling laws and data efficiency of MLLMs. As highlighted in recent surveys <A survey on multimodal large language models>, the primary goal of these interfaces is to align the representation spaces of vision and language without disrupting the rich semantic knowledge embedded within the frozen LLM. For instance, linear projectors often struggle with high-resolution inputs due to the fixed context window of the LLM, whereas token-based methods like <EMU> or deep fusion like <FIBER> are designed to handle variable-length or interleaved inputs more naturally.

This evolution reflects a fundamental tension between efficiency and expressive power. For instance, while linear projectors offer minimal latency, they often struggle with the semantic misalignment inherent in the modality gap. In contrast, more complex connectors like Q-Former introduce significant parameter overhead but enable better feature compression <BLIP-2>. This trade-off drives research toward hybrid approaches that seek the best of both worlds, such as the decoupled visual pathway in <mPLUG-Owl2>. These architectural choices are not merely technical details; they fundamentally shape the model's data efficiency and scaling laws, determining how well the interface aligns representation spaces without disrupting the rich semantic knowledge embedded within the frozen LLM.

#### 2.1 Linear Projectors

The foundational approach to bridging the modality gap relies on simple linear projection layers. <LLaVA> first demonstrated the viability of this method by connecting a visual encoder to an LLM using a single linear layer, establishing a baseline for parameter efficiency. This approach aligns visual features into the language embedding space with minimal overhead, though it often struggles with the significant distributional discrepancy between modalities. However, when combined with high-quality instruction tuning data, such simple projectors can yield impressive multimodal capabilities. While highly efficient, these linear methods typically lack the capacity to compress or restructure visual information deeply, often leading to suboptimal performance on complex tasks requiring fine-grained visual understanding.

#### 2.2 Connector-Based Architectures

To address the limitations of simple projectors, a prominent approach involves the use of specialized modules to map visual tokens into the semantic space of the LLM. This section examines two dominant paradigms: Q-Former based approaches and Perceiver-based approaches. These architectures effectively act as 'bottlenecks' or 'compressors', attempting to distill the most relevant visual information into a fixed number of tokens that the LLM can process efficiently.

##### 2.2.1 Q-Former based Approaches

<MiniGPT-4> utilizes Q-Former (inspired by <BLIP-2>) to learn a set of learnable queries (typically 32) that aggregate visual features from a frozen ViT before they are fed into the LLM. This method proved that treating visual input as a sequence of compressed, highly relevant features allows an otherwise frozen LLM to understand complex modalities, unlocking emergent capabilities like website generation from sketches. The mechanism of Q-Former in <MiniGPT-4> is particularly noteworthy for its role as a feature bottleneck. By freezing the visual encoder and training only the query vectors and the projection layer, the model learns to extract the most relevant visual information conditioned on the learnable queries. Internally, Q-Former employs a dual cross-attention mechanism: one set of cross-attention layers interacts the learnable queries with the image features to perform visual compression, while another set interacts the queries with text embeddings to facilitate multimodal fusion. This process effectively reduces the sequence length of visual tokens (e.g., from thousands of patch features to 32 queries), which significantly lowers the computational burden on the subsequent LLM. However, this aggressive compression can lead to information loss, especially for tasks requiring fine-grained spatial understanding. This contrasts with methods like Perceiver-based architectures, which initialize latent tokens directly from the input sequence and iteratively refine them, rather than relying on static, learnable queries.

The foundational training of Q-Former is heavily influenced by the BLIP-2 framework, which utilizes a mixture of three key objectives: Image-Text Contrastive (ITC) loss to align image and text representations in a shared space, ITM loss to fine-grained alignment via binary classification, and Masked Language Modeling (MLM) loss to predict masked text tokens conditioned on visual features. Specifically, the ITC objective aligns the representation of the [CLS] token from the text encoder with the pooled output of the visual queries, ensuring a global correspondence between the image and the caption. The ITM objective acts as a fine-grained filter, using a binary classifier to determine if a pair of image and text is matched, which helps the model distinguish between positive and negative pairs with high precision. Finally, the MLM objective enables the model to learn cross-modal relationships by masking text tokens and requiring the model to predict them using the information provided by the visual queries. This objective is particularly crucial for the 'feature bottleneck' concept: by forcing the 32 queries to reconstruct masked text, it compels them to retain the semantic information necessary for language generation, rather than just raw visual features. This multi-objective approach trains the queries to distill the most semantically relevant information for language generation. <BLIVA> attempts to mitigate the information loss of pure Q-Former approaches by retaining raw patch embeddings alongside the compressed queries, thereby preserving high-resolution visual details for text-rich images. This hybrid strategy effectively addresses the limitations of pure compression, proving particularly effective for tasks requiring fine-grained visual understanding of text or intricate details.

##### 2.2.2 Perceiver-based Approaches

Similarly, <KOSMOS-1> adopts a Perceiver-like architecture to handle interleaved multimodal inputs within a single Transformer. This architecture utilizes a cross-attention mechanism to iteratively compress input sequences into a fixed set of latent tokens. This design allows it to process variable-length multimodal data efficiently. By employing a mapping network and special boundary tokens, it effectively unifies modalities. However, reliance on complex, pre-trained feature compressors introduces significant parameter overhead and latency. In contrast to the single-pass aggregation of Q-Former, the Perceiver in <KOSMOS-1> uses a recurrent cross-attention mechanism that iteratively refines a set of latent tokens against the full visual input. Specifically, the Perceiver architecture consists of a repeating block of cross-attention layers followed by self-attention layers. In the cross-attention layer, the latent tokens (queries) attend to the visual features (keys/values), allowing the model to distill information from the high-resolution visual input into the compressed latent space. Subsequently, self-attention layers among the latent tokens allow them to communicate and integrate the gathered information. This process is repeated multiple times, progressively refining the latent representation. This iterative approach allows it to maintain a richer representation of the visual scene, albeit at the cost of increased inference time. This architectural difference in training objectives is also notable: while Q-Former relies on the BLIP-2 objectives (ITC, ITM, MLM) to align compressed features with language semantics, Perceiver-based methods in architectures like <KOSMOS-1> typically optimize a reconstruction loss (e.g., Mean Squared Error) on the visual features, prioritizing the retention of low-level visual fidelity over semantic alignment with language, which fundamentally shapes their respective compression strategies.

Comparative analysis reveals distinct performance trade-offs. While Q-Former-based connectors like <MiniGPT-4> offer lower latency and parameter counts, they may struggle with complex visual reasoning that requires detailed spatial awareness. This is largely due to their aggressive compression strategy, which reduces the sequence length of visual tokens (e.g., from thousands of patch features to 32 queries), significantly lowering the computational burden on the subsequent LLM but risking information loss. The Perceiver-based approach in <KOSMOS-1> provides better handling of variable-length inputs and potentially richer feature integration, but introduces higher computational costs due to iterative cross-attention layers. This comparative analysis highlights an ongoing design tension:
- **Compression vs. Fidelity**: Balancing the need for efficient, compressed representations that fit within LLM context windows against the necessity of preserving sufficient visual fidelity for complex reasoning tasks.
- **Latency vs. Integration**: Balancing the lower latency of single-pass aggregation against the richer feature integration (and higher cost) of iterative methods.

This trade-off between compression efficiency and computational cost defines a key tension in early connector designs. While effective, these static compression methods risk information loss, motivating a shift towards dynamic integration.

#### 2.3 Deep Fusion Mechanisms

While the connector-based architectures discussed above rely on static compression to bridge the modality gap, recent research has shifted towards a paradigm of deep fusion. This shift is motivated by the limitations of static pre-fusion, where aggressive compression strategies (e.g., reducing thousands of visual patches to a few dozen queries in Q-Former) risk significant information loss, as noted in analyses of connector-based architectures <BLIP-2>. Consequently, deep fusion seeks to integrate vision and language more deeply within the model backbone, enabling dynamic interaction throughout the generation process. <mPLUG-Owl2> introduces a "modality-adaptive module" that preserves modality-specific features, arguing that strict fusion can degrade text-only capabilities. It decouples the visual pathway while still sharing a universal language decoder. Conversely, <FIBER> argues for "Fusion in the Backbone." Instead of a separate connector, it interleaves cross-attention layers directly into the LLM’s encoder and decoder context. This deep fusion allows the model to reason about visual dependencies dynamically throughout the generation process, rather than relying on a static pre-fusion step. This trade-off—between specialized, compressed encoders (<MiniGPT-4>) and deep, integrated fusion (<FIBER>)—defines the current tension in interface design. Specifically, <mPLUG-Owl2> utilizes a "Modality-Adaptive Gating" mechanism within its cross-attention layers, allowing the model to selectively attend to visual or textual inputs depending on the current token, thereby maintaining modality integrity. In contrast, <FIBER> replaces standard self-attention layers in the LLM backbone with cross-attention layers that query visual tokens at every layer. Architecturally, this means the Key and Value (K, V) matrices are derived exclusively from visual tokens, while the Query (Q) matrix comes from the text token representations. This forces the model to ground every text token generation on visual evidence, rather than allowing text tokens to attend only to previous text tokens (autoregressive self-attention). This shift has profound implications for training dynamics: by bypassing the standard self-attention mechanism, <FIBER> may alter the gradient flow, potentially requiring careful tuning to maintain stability and prevent the degradation of the LLM's linguistic reasoning capabilities. This architectural change may necessitate specialized initialization strategies or learning rate schedules to ensure stable convergence, a challenge less pronounced in connector-based approaches <FIBER>. This enables "early fusion," where visual context is available at every token generation step, allowing visual information to influence the generation of every token, not just the initial context, enabling a more dynamic and fine-grained interaction where visual reasoning is interleaved with linguistic generation.

#### 2.4 Token-Based Methods

In contrast to the deep fusion approach, which integrates modalities via dynamic cross-attention within the model's processing layers, another distinct paradigm seeks to unify the input space itself by treating visual data as a sequence of tokens. This trend diverges into two primary applications: generative modeling of interleaved sequences and task translation for structured outputs. These methods treat visual information as a sequence of tokens directly compatible with the LLM's processing pipeline, moving beyond simple projection to a unified token space. Conceptually, this represents a shift from the dynamic, interleaved reasoning of deep fusion (e.g., <FIBER>) to a static unification of the input representation, where vision is treated as a 'foreign language' natively understood by the LLM (e.g., <VisionLLM>). The first application focuses on generative modeling, while the second focuses on translating visual tasks into a language-like format for execution.

##### 2.4.1 Discrete Tokenization

This approach focuses on representing images as discrete codes for generative modeling. It involves mapping visual patches to a discrete codebook or generating visual tokens via a specialized tokenizer, allowing the model to process visual inputs much like text. <EMU> exemplifies this by utilizing a visual tokenizer to convert images into discrete tokens, which are then interleaved with text tokens in a unified sequence for generative modeling. These methods effectively treat vision as a "foreign language" that the LLM can natively understand.

##### 2.4.2 Unified Input Spaces

This approach focuses on translating visual tasks into a language-like format for task execution. A notable evolution of this concept is found in <VisionLLM>, which proposes treating visual data as a "foreign language" via a generative decoder. This is achieved by translating diverse visual tasks into a language-like format: for instance, object detection is reformulated as a prompt such as "Detect <object>". The LLM then generates a structured sequence of special tokens that are decoded into structured outputs like bounding boxes or segmentation masks. This strategy differs from <EMU>'s focus on interleaved generation of text and image sequences; instead, <VisionLLM> focuses on a unified generative framework for handling diverse visual tasks. This strategy simplifies the architecture by removing the need for complex, modality-specific connectors, aligning with the trend toward architectural unification.

#### 2.5 Architectural Impact on Data Efficiency and Training Paradigms

The efficacy of these architectural designs is intrinsically linked to the training data and paradigms they require. This illustrates a broader trend: as architectures evolve from simple linear layers to complex, deep fusion mechanisms, they often demand more specialized and extensive training data to unlock their full potential. For instance, simple linear projectors like those in <LLaVA> can often achieve strong performance with pure instruction-tuning data, whereas connector-based architectures like <BLIP-2> typically benefit from a two-stage training pipeline involving large-scale pre-training on image-text pairs to learn the mapping between modalities before instruction tuning <2306.13549>. Deep fusion models like <FIBER> require interleaved image-text data during pre-training to effectively learn the cross-attention mechanisms, whereas simpler projectors can rely solely on instruction-tuning data. This dependency highlights that architectural complexity must be matched by data complexity to realize advanced capabilities. Specifically, while connector-based models often leverage massive web-scale datasets like LAION-5B for pre-training the compression module, deep fusion models necessitate curated interleaved corpora (e.g., from WebLI or internal datasets) to train the integrated cross-attention layers effectively. This shift implies that as models move towards deeper integration, the data requirement shifts from simple image-text pairs to more complex, sequential multimodal data, significantly increasing the cost and complexity of pre-training.

Beyond the sheer volume and structure of data, the training paradigms themselves are evolving to address the specific challenges posed by these advanced interfaces. A prominent trend is the move towards **decoupled training strategies**. For instance, models like <BLIP-2> and <MiniGPT-4> freeze the visual encoder and the LLM, training only the lightweight connector (Q-Former) and a projection layer. This approach is highly parameter-efficient and prevents the catastrophic forgetting of the pre-trained knowledge within the powerful LLM and vision encoder. However, it can limit the model's ability to develop deeply integrated, cross-modal representations. In contrast, deep fusion models like <FIBER> often require **full fine-tuning** or at least tuning of the entire cross-attention mechanism within the LLM backbone. While this can lead to superior performance on complex reasoning tasks that require tight interleaving of modalities, it is computationally expensive and risks degrading the LLM's original linguistic capabilities if not carefully managed. This trade-off between parameter efficiency and representational power defines a central tension in modern MLLM training. Furthermore, the rise of **reinforcement learning from human feedback (RLHF)** and its multimodal extension, **RLHF-V**, is beginning to influence interface design. These methods can refine the model's outputs to be more helpful and harmless, but they place different demands on the interface. A highly compressed interface might lose the subtle visual details necessary for accurate reward modeling, whereas a more expressive interface could provide the granularity needed for fine-grained preference learning. Therefore, the choice of training paradigm—be it parameter-efficient tuning, full fine-tuning, or RLHF-based alignment—is not independent of the architectural design but is deeply intertwined with it, co-determining the final capabilities and limitations of the MLLM.

#### 2.6 Output Interfaces and Grounding

Parallel to the evolution of input interfaces, the output interfaces have also evolved to enable more structured and grounded responses, moving beyond pure text generation. A prominent direction is explicit visual grounding, where generated text is spatially anchored to the visual input. <KOSMOS-2> exemplifies this by altering the output interface to align text with specific visual regions, moving beyond internal processing to produce spatially-aware outputs. Similarly, <VisionLLM> proposes treating visual data as a "foreign language" to generate structured outputs for diverse tasks like detection and segmentation, effectively unifying the generative interface across modalities. Specifically, <VisionLLM> decodes special tokens into structured coordinates, while <KOSMOS-2> generates <loc> and <ref> tokens to link text spans to bounding boxes.

#### 2.7 Specialized Adaptations and Robustness Challenges

This evolution is further driven by the need to address domain-specific limitations. For example, <BLIVA> addresses the failure of generalist connectors in text-rich images by explicitly injecting patch embeddings alongside learned query embeddings, suggesting that "one size fits all" interfaces may not be optimal. As the line between the visual encoder and the language model blurs, exemplified by the proposal for visual extraction tuning in <Downscaling Intelligence: Exploring Perception and Reasoning Bottlenecks in Small Multimodal Models>, the modality interface is effectively becoming part of the reasoning engine itself, rather than a mere data conduit. This shift is critical, as recent benchmarks like <BLINK: Multimodal Large Language Models Can See but Not Perceive> and <BLINK-Twice: You see, but do you observe?> reveal that despite architectural advances, MLLMs still struggle with core visual perception and observation, often relying on linguistic shortcuts rather than true visual reasoning. This underscores that the ultimate goal of the modality interface is not just alignment, but enabling genuine visual understanding.

#### 2.8 Summary

The evolution of modality interfaces traces a clear trajectory from static, simple linear projections to dynamic, deeply integrated fusion mechanisms. This progression is fundamentally driven by the need to bridge the modality gap while balancing the critical trade-offs between computational efficiency and expressive power. Early linear projectors offered minimal overhead but struggled with semantic misalignment, while connector-based architectures like Q-Former introduced learnable compression at the cost of potential information loss and parameter overhead. The subsequent shift towards deep fusion mechanisms, such as those in <FIBER>, enables more dynamic and interleaved reasoning but at a higher computational cost. Finally, token-based methods aim for a unified input space, treating vision as a native language for the LLM.

#### 2.9 Future Outlook

Looking ahead, the trajectory of modality interfaces points toward greater unification and dynamic interaction. The tension between efficiency and expressiveness remains a central challenge, suggesting that future architectures will likely be hybrid, combining the strengths of different stages—for instance, leveraging efficient compression for initial context while enabling deep fusion for complex reasoning steps. Furthermore, as the interface becomes more integrated into the reasoning engine itself, the line between perception and reasoning will continue to blur. This evolution necessitates a deeper understanding of how these architectural choices impact not just performance on benchmarks, but also the model's fundamental capabilities in spatial reasoning, visual grounding, and robustness against adversarial attacks. The next section will explore how these advanced interface designs enable complex multimodal reasoning capabilities.
